Preface
The current study presents the Master Thesis conducted by Sophie Stride Geyti in collaboration
with the Department of Environmental and Resource Engineering at the Technical University of
Denmark. The project was conducted in relation with the European project, AshCycle - Integra-
tion of Underutilized Ashes into Material Cycles by Industry-Urban Symbiosis, by examining the
possibilities of utilizing sewage sludge ash in compressed earth blocks.
The thesis was carried out in the period from 02.01.2023 to the 02.06.23, and constitutes for 30
ECTS, as part of the fulfillment for the degree of Master of Science in Architectural Engineer-
ing. The project was supervised by Associate Professor Gunvor Marie Kirkelund and Postdoc Ida
Maria Gieysztor Bertelsen.
Sophie Stride Geyti

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
There is a growing interest in finding sustainable building materials, to mitigate the depletion
of natural resources and climate change. Currently, both sewage sludge ash (SSA) and soil are
deposited on landfill, not utilizing its full potential. SSA contains valuable nutrients and metals,
that can be extracted, which leaves a residue similar to silica sand, that could be utilized in different
building materials (Ottosen et al. (2015). Furthermore, research indicate that SSA can stabilize
soil due to a pozzolanic reaction between the SSA and clay, improving the properties of earth
building materials (Aparna, 2022).
This study investigates whether SSA can be utilized in combination with clay loam, focusing on
whether it can improve the mechanical and physical performance of unstabilized compressed earth
blocks (CEB).
Four different clay loams were characterized to find the one suitable for production of CEB. The
following tests were conducted: Loss on ignition, density of solid particles, particle size distri-
bution, Atterberg limits, linear shrinkage and XRD to identify the clay minerals. The SSA was
characterized by particle size distribution, digestion and leaching.
A red clay loam from Egernsund-Wienerberger was found suitable when mixed with additional
sand. To examine the potential of the SSA, the loam was combined with the following binders:
5% SSA, 5%SSA + 5% CaO, 5% CaO, 5% CaO + 5% inert quartz (Qz). The clay loam itself was
included as reference. The SSA was used in its raw format, thus nutrients and heavy metals had
not been removed. Cylindrical specimens of Ø60, H60 was cured at two different temperatures,
23°C, and 40°C for 14 days, with 7 days of drying afterwards at 23°C, 65%RH.
The mechanical performance of the different soil mixes was determined by testing the optimal
moisture content, the compressive strength and hygrothermal performance in terms of sorption
isotherms. The adsorption and desorption of the specimens were conducted in a climate chamber
at 33%RH, 75%RH and 94%RH. To be able to consider the environmental aspect of using SSA in
CEB, leaching of the different soils was also examined.
The findings of this thesis indicated that the SSA did not impair the mechanical properties. The
iv
==PAGE:6==
reference soil and the 5% SSA reached the highest compressive strengths and the SSA reduced the
shrinkage. The soil might have been of too good quality, and the curing time too short, to see the
effect of SSA as a stabilizing agent. The results therefore mainly indicates that SSA can be used
as a filler or light aggregate, with the option of substituting sand in CEB. The leaching was found
to be limited.

Introduction
The construction sector has been identified as one of the sectors which contributes the most to
climate change. Focus has therefore recently moved towards finding sustainable building materials
to reduce the energy demand, carbon emission and depletion of natural resources (Reddy, 2022;
Fabbri et al., 2022). One way to mitigate the challenges in relation with the construction industry,
is to use low-embodied carbon materials and to utilize waste (Reddy, 2022).
In 2020 in Denmark alone, the construction industry accounted for 40% of the total waste gener-
ated, which corresponds to 5 million tonnes waste, when soil is not included. Including soil, the
waste from construction was 11 million tonnes. 24% of the contaminated soil were deposited as
well as 14% of the uncontaminated soil, because the demand was too small. The waste generation
from sludge from wastewater treatment was 115,000 tonnes, where 82% was utilized as fertilizer
in agriculture, 13% was incinerated and 4% was deposited (Kiilerich et al., 2020).
At the moment sewage sludge ash (SSA) contains valuable nutrients for agriculture such as phos-
phourus, but the phosphorous is currently bound to the ash, and thereby not accessible for plants.
Research is examining a way to extract the phosphourus, leaving a residue of SSA which is similar
to silica sand. If SSA could be utilized in its full potential, it would benefit the environment, as the
extraction of nutrients would benefit the agriculture and the silica sand residue could be used in
other applications, e.g. as a construction material. By utilizing SSA, it would no longer be neces-
sary to deposit the waste product, and it could reduce the depletion of natural resources (Ottosen
et al., 2015).
Additionally, there have been a long tradition, also within Europe, of building with earth (Reddy,
2022). By building residential houses and dwellings with unfired clay bricks, the carbon emission
would significantly be lowered compared to masonry and other conventional building materials
(Malkanthi et al., 2020). But the durability and strength of earth building materials are much
lower. A way to comprehend these problems is to stabilize the soil by using mechanical and
chemical stabilization (Malkanthi et al., 2020). Research have shown promising results within
stabilization of soft cohesive subgrade soil by using SSA and by utilizing SSA as a fill material
within earth construction (Dhir et al., 2017a; Aparna, 2022; Lin et al., 2007; Kadhim et al., 2022).
1
==PAGE:9==
Aim and scope
The objective of this thesis is therefore to examine whether it is possible to work out a sustainable
construction material, focusing on compressed earth blocks (CEB), by combining clayey soil with
sewage sludge ash (SSA), and examine whether the material properties are enhanced when SSA
is used for soil stabilization.
The thesis will not consider other types of earth building materials, such as rammed earth, adobe,
cob, and wattle and daub, or earth building materials with the addition of fibers, and will neither
go into the regulations of using CEB as construction elements for load-bearing walls.
Sustainability Development Goals
The subject of this thesis target SDG 12 listed by the United Nations Department of Economic and
Social Affairs.
Figure 1.1: SDG 12 (United Nations, n.d.)
Goal number 12 strives to ensure sustainable consumption and production patterns, where the
thesis relates to the two targets 12.2 and 12.5 by looking into utilizing SSA as a filler and stabilizing
agent for possible substitution of sand, cement and lime.
12.2
"By 2030, achieve the sustainable management and effi-
cient use of natural resources."
12.5
"By 2030, substantially reduce waste generation through
prevention, reduction, recycling and reuse."
(United Nations, n.d.)

Theory
2.1
Building with earth
Our ancestors used soil for building construction because of local availability, cost, and the fact that
the technology were not developed to process materials as known today, thus earth as a construc-
tion material can be considered less energy intensive compared to conventional building materials
(Reddy, 2022).
Today, modern earth structures can be grouped into two broad categories of 1) unstabilized earth
structures and 2) stabilized earth structures. The natural soil contains clay, which acts as a binder
of the soil, but the clay can also cause swelling and shrinkage in unstabilized earth structures due
to moisture movement. To prevent this, the soil can be stabilized mechanically by adding inert
materials, such as sand, gravel, and aggregates, so the content of clay comes to an optimum level.
Mechanical stabilization is used in both categories of modern earth structures, where the earth-
building material is densified through compaction. Stabilized earth structures also use inorganic
stabilizer such as cement and lime, which can increase the strength and durability of the building
material (Reddy, 2022).
2.1.1
Compressed earth blocks
There exists different techniques of building with earth, e.g. adobe, cob, wattle and daub, rammed
earth and compressed earth blocks (CEB). This thesis considers the technique of CEB.
CEB are unfired bricks or blocks produced by compacting humid earth manually or by using a
hydraulic press. CEB can both be used when it is unstabilized or when it is stabilized by adding
a low content of binder to the earth. The most common stabilizers are currently cement and lime.
A masonry wall can then be built by using a mortar of the material with or without the binder that
stabilizes the CEB (Fabbri et al., 2022).
When building with CEB, the embodied energy can be reduced compared to conventional building
materials. This is indicated in figure 2.1 from Reddy (2022).
3
==PAGE:11==
Figure 2.1: A comparison of the embodied energy of a buildings life cycle when built with different
materials (Reddy, 2022).
From figure 2.1 it is illustrated that the embodied energy of using CEB (Building C) is much lower
than the embodied energy of using reinforced concrete with clay brick masonry (Building E).
Furthermore, it is possible to reduce the carbon emission when building with CEB instead of
conventional building materials. The embodied carbon of a residential building with RC frame
and infill masonry or a monolithic RC construction is 400-600 kgCO2eq/m2, while the embodied
carbon of a building with cement stabilized CEB, geopolymer or lime-pozzolana binder bricks is
150-200 kgCO2eq/m2 (Reddy, 2022). Hence there are some environmental gains when using CEB
as a construction material in e.g. dwellings and residential buildings.
2.1.2
Clay minerals and soil composition
Clay minerals is the soils natural binder (Minke, 2012). The clay can make the soil shrink and
swell depending on the clay minerals, but its internal friction and cohesion also make the soil
gain strength (Reddy, 2022). The binding force and compressive strength depends on the type of
clay minerals that are present in the soil, and the quantity of cations (Minke, 2012). Additionally,
does the clay contain silica and alumina, that can react with the binders added to the soil creating
hydration products (Lin et al., 2007; Reddy, 2022).
4
==PAGE:12==
It is mainly smectites such as montmorillonite and vermiculites, that has a tendency to swell,
whereas it depends on the type of kaolinite, whether it can swell. Illite do not swell (Brindley,
1981; Lugwisha, 2011).
Figure 2.2: Structure and spacing of the three most common clay minerals (Minke, 2012)
The figure above shows the structure of the three most common clay minerals as presented in
Minke (2012). Each layer consists of a aluminum hydroxide (the dark layer) and a layer of silicon
oxide (the white layer). The kaolinite is double layered, with a low ion-binding capacity and a
d-spacing of 7Å, where montmorillonite consists of three layers, where one aluminum hydroxide
is always covered by two silicon oxide layers, which gives a higher ion binding capacity. The d-
spacing of montmorillonite is between 14-20Å, and the d-spacing of illite is 10Å (Minke, 2012).
The d-spacing of montmorillonite can change because of its swelling properties (Brindley, 1981).
When clay work as the binder of the soil, silt and sand work as the filler (Minke, 2012).
Standards and codes suggest the following soil composition and soil properties when working with
stabilized CEB (Reddy, 2022):
Table 2.1: Earth and soil composition and soil properties specified in different standards/codes on stabilized
CEB (Reddy, 2022)
Composition parameter
Stabilized CEB
Clay
2-30%
Sand (0.075-0.425mm)
>60%
Gravel
0-10%
Liquid limit
≤45%
Plasticity index
≤12%
pH
6-8.5
5
==PAGE:13==
The clay content can indicate what type of stabilizer and the amount that is suitable for the soil.
A clay rich on montmorillonite favour lime stabilization, whereas a clay rich on kaolinite favours
cement stabilization. The more clay, the more stabilizing agent can be used (Reddy, 2022; Minke,
2012). Stabilization of CEB is further explained in section 2.1.4.4.
2.1.3
Hygrothermal performance
Next to sustainability, the hygrothermal performance of earth-based materials is an argument for
building with earth. Earth-based materials have a high potential of enhancing the thermal comfort
of dwellings, regulating the indoor climate, which creates a healthier and more comfortable living
environment as well as reducing the energy used to create a comfortable temperature (McGregor
et al., 2014; Fabbri et al., 2022).
The hygroscopicity of earth-based materials is based on the porous network, which enables water
vapour to enter and leave the material. The porous network is created as clay, silt, and sand does
not connect perfectly, leaving pores inside the material. Clay itself is a porous medium, giving the
earth-based building material a sort of double-porosity, enhancing the hygrothermal performance
of the material compared to other conventional materials (Fabbri et al., 2022). Because the hy-
grothermal performance depends on the materials ability to relase and absorb water, it depends
on the type and content of clay and whether it is stabilized or not (Fabbri et al., 2022). Another
quality of earth-based building materials is that they are faster at reaching equilibrium moisture
content than other building materials (Liuzzi et al., 2013).
El Fgaier et al. (2016) and Wiehle et al. (2022) found one disadvantage of the high hygroscopicity
of earth-based materials. When the relative humidity (RH) is increased, the strength of the earth
is reduced.
One way to examine the hygrothermal performance of materials is by looking at sorption isotherms,
that show the materials equilibrium moisture content at steady state, thus the materials ability to
absorp and release water vapour at a constant temperature.
El Fgaier et al. (2016) tested the sorption capacity of three types of unfired clay bricks. The unfired
clay bricks reached a moisture content of 3.5% at 95% RH. The sorption isotherms can be seen in
figure 2.3.
6
==PAGE:14==
Figure 2.3: Sorption isotherms of unfired clay bricks from El Fgaier et al. (2016)
El Fgaier et al. (2016) attribute the difference in sorption capacity to the type of clay mineral and
the specific surface area of the soil, as the Brick B contained montmorillonite.
Minke (2012) presents in figure 2.4 different sorption isotherms for loams and clays.
7
==PAGE:15==
Figure 2.4: Equilibrium moisture content for different loams and clays (Minke, 2012)
The sorption isotherms support the conclusion by El Fgaier et al. (2016) as the water content of
bentonite (7), which contained 70% montmorillonite reached the highest water content, and the
non-expansive clay kaolinite (6) reached the lowest water content. Furthermore, Minke (2012)
shows in figure 2.4, that the loam brick reached a higher water content than different types of
loam. Of the different types of loams, the silty loam (2) reached a higher water content than the
sandy loam (3), the clayey loam (1), and the granular clayey loam (4).
8
==PAGE:16==
Figure 2.5: Sorption isotherm for concrete, w/c
= 0.66, degree of hydration = 96%, 237 kg ce-
ment pr. m3 (Gottfredsen & Nielsen, 2015)
Figure 2.6: Sorption isotherm for fired bricks,
𝜌= 1860 kg/m3 (Gottfredsen & Nielsen, 2015)
Figure 2.5 and 2.6 show the sorption isotherm of the two conventional building materials, concrete
and fired bricks. The sorption isotherm of concrete highly depends on the amount of cement used,
the degree of hydration and the w/c ratio, thus figure 2.5 is an example of what a sorption isotherm
of concrete can look like. With this example, the concrete reached a higher moisture content than
the loams in El Fgaier et al. (2016) and Minke (2012), while the fired bricks only reached a
moisture content of 0.7% at 98% RH, which is much lower compared to both the concrete and the
loams. All materials show the hysteresis phenomenon, which indicates that it is easier for water
vapour to enter the material than to leave it (El Fgaier et al., 2016).
2.1.4
Production method of CEB
The production method can have a large influence on the final product. Care must therefore be
taken on the preparation, mixing, soil composition, compaction, curing conditions, and stabiliza-
tion of the soil.
9
==PAGE:17==
2.1.4.1
Soil preparation and mixing
Houben & Guillaud (1994) give the following guideline on how to prepare excavated soil to pro-
duce CEB:
Grinding and
pulverization
Grinding or pulverization is a necessary process to be able to obtain a uniform
mixing of mineral components, water and stabilizer. Before grinding or pulver-
ization, lumps greater than 200mm must be broken up. Stones and gravel are
left intact, while soil with clay is broken up so it only contains grains <5mm.
The soil must be dry as wet soil can only be handled by some mechanized sys-
tems.
Screening
Large elements and organic matter must be removed. Grains with a diameter
between 10-20mm are allowed.
Mixing
Mixing can be performed either manually or mechanically. A uniform mixture
must be obtained, regardless of whether a stabilizer is used or not.
Manually: Turn over the heap of soil at least 4 times.
Mechanical mixer: 3 to 4 minutes is sufficient.
Water is added to the dry soil by a sprinkler or mist sprayer.
It is not recommended to use a conventional concrete mixer, because of the
formation of lumps and crumbs in the soil.
Minke (2012) suggests a mixing time of 10 minutes, as it was discovered that the binding force
of silty mud mortar increased by 57%, compared to when mixed for one minute. But there is also
an optimum mixing time. It was found that when mixing for 20 minutes the binding force was
reduced with 11%. The binding force can be examined by dropping a ball of mixed soil at its
plastic limit from a height of 2m. The ball with less binding force will deform more and have
more cracks after the drop (Minke, 2012).
2.1.4.2
Modifying the soil composition
If the soil is too rich in clay, the soil composition can be modified by adding sand or gravel, which
have been wetted before being mixed into the soil. The addition of sand or gravel can increase the
strength. By thinning the loam, the shrinkage is also reduced (Minke, 2012).
10
==PAGE:18==
2.1.4.3
Moulding and compaction
Moulding and compacting the soil is done to increase the density and friction, which improves the
mechanical strength of the soil. The degree of compaction depends on the grading, particle mor-
phology, organic content, moisture content and compaction effort of the soil (Dhir et al., 2017a).
Before moulding, uncompacted soil has a density between 1000 and 1400 kg/m3 (Houben & Guil-
laud, 1994). To achieve the desired density (minimum 1700 kg/m3 or in the region of the Proctor
value), the compression ratio should be 2, which is the ratio of the height of the mould before
compression to that after compression. When compressing the specimen, the density will not be
uniformly distributed. The density is higher at the moving compaction plate than at the static plate
(Fabbri et al., 2022). This rises a variability of the quality, thus the thickness of the final product
should not be greater than 9-10 cm when low pressure is used (Houben & Guillaud, 1994).
The hold-back time between mixing and moulding can have a large influence on the quality of
the final product. For cement stabilization, the hold-back time should be as little as possible,
as a delay of one to two hours can result in a premature settlement. Whereas, when using lime
stabilization, the quality of the blocks may be enhanced by a longer hold-back time, as the lime is
slow at reacting with the air (Houben & Guillaud, 1994).
The optimal moisture content (OMC) can be determined by the Proctor method, but according to
Boussaa et al. (2023) and Abbou et al. (2020), the Proctor method is inappropriate for determining
the OMC when working with CEB, as the compaction when using Proctor is very different from
the compaction method used for manufacturing CEB (Abbou et al., 2020). It is, therefore more
suitable to determine the water content by applying static compression using a hydraulic press
(Boussaa et al., 2023). Boussaa et al. (2023) compacted moulds with 10 MPa, and Abbou et
al. (2020) compacted the specimens with 3 MPa at a speed of 1.27mm/min. Before compaction,
Abbou et al. (2020) mixed the soil and binder with water in a kneader for 15 minutes to make
the mixture homogeneous. Afterwards, the mixture was sealed for 24 hours, for the water to
homogeneously redistribute. Furthermore, Abbou et al. (2020) found that the OMC increased,
and the dry density decreased, when the lime content increased. When using 2-6% lime, the OMC
was in the range of 16-20% water.
11
==PAGE:19==
2.1.4.4
Stabilization of CEB
Stabilization can be added to increase the strength and durability of the CEB. The pozzolanic
reaction from the lime and clay produces cementitious products, which contributes to the strength
development. The most common stabilizers in earth construction is cement and lime, but other
pozzolanic additives such as fly ash, silica fume, ground blast furnace slag, rice husk ash and
volcanic ash have also been used in combination with lime to improve the strength (Reddy, 2022).
The type of clay determine which stabilizer is useful. Non-expansive clays such as kaolinite
benefit from cement stabilization, while expansive clays such as montmorillonite benefit from lime
stabilization, as the lime will reduce the shrinkage and swelling behaviour (Reddy, 2022; Minke,
2012). In addition, litterature have shown that cement stabilization does not have an optimum,
thus adding more cement will increase the strength, but if using too much lime, the lime will work
as a lubricant between the soil particles and reduce the strength (Reddy, 2022). Therefore, Reddy
(2022) found in literature that the optimum lime content is between 8-12.5% depending on the
clay content and type. Higher content of clay requires more lime, and the expansive clay minerals
such as montmorillonite can absorb more lime than the non-expansive clay minerals. If the clay
content is very high, it is suggested to thin the soil beforehand to reduce the needed lime, as it
would be a more economical and viable solution (Reddy, 2022).
A study by Abbou et al. (2020) used 2%, 4%, and 6% lime for stabilizing a soil mixture of clay
and crushed sand. This resulted in a compressive strength of 5.6 MPa, when using 6% lime. The
clay content of the soil was 30%.
A study by Malkanthi et al. (2020) tested a soil with a low clay and silt content, which was
stabilized with lime (5%, 10%, and 15%), cement (8%, and 10%) and a combination of lime and
cement adding up to 10% binder (3/7%, 5/5%, 7/3%). The study found that the most optimal
percentage was combining 5% lime and 5% cement.
2.1.4.5
Curing conditions
When working with lime as a stabilizer, the stabilized CEB must be cured to facilitate the poz-
zoloanic reaction between the lime and clay minerals. The reaction is slow at ambient tempera-
tures, thus the curing condition influences the final strength. Curing at higher temperatures will
increase the strength. Curing can furthermore be done either as moist curing or steam curing
(Reddy, 2022). When using cement as a stabilizer, the curing time is 28 days, but when only using
12
==PAGE:20==
lime, the curing time may be longer. The same accounts for SSA (Lin et al., 2007). After curing,
the stabilized CEB should dry before being used in construction (Reddy, 2022).
2.2
Sewage sludge ash
Sewage sludge ash is a residue from wastewater treatment that contains heavy metals, pathogens,
and bacteria. It also contains valuable nutrients and organic matter such as phosphorous and nitro-
gen. Particularly phosphorous are of special interest within agriculture, as the nutrient is important
for plants and agriculture. Phosphorous appears on the EU’s list of critical raw materials (European
Commission, 2020; European Commission, n.d.), by which SSA is of interest as it contains 5-10%
phosphorous, which is as high as in mined materials. The phosphorous in SSA is bound to iron or
aluminum when phosphor is deposited during wastewater treatment, but ferrous phosphate is not
very soluble and therefore not plant available, thus the SSA cannot be used directly as a fertilizer
or soil improver in agriculture at the moment. Research is looking into extracting the phosphorus
by electrodialytic separation or via Ash2Phos. Electrodialytic separation is at TRL level 4, while
Ash2Phos is at TRL level 5-6. After extracting the phosphorus, the SSA becomes like silica sand.
Until the processes are commercialized, SSA is deposited, waiting to be utilized. Research shows
that SSA potentially can be utilized within construction materials, either as a filler/aggregate or as
supplementary cementitious material due to its pozzolanic reactivity (Ottosen et al., 2013; Payá et
al., 2019).
2.2.1
Soil improvement by using SSA
Research shows that basic soil properties, such as compaction, shear strength, and bearing capacity
are improved when using SSA or hydrated lime as stabilizers in soil. The soil improvement can be
a result of a pozzolanic reaction from the sewage sludge ash and lime, where the calcium in both
products produces calcium silicate hydrates or aluminum silicate hydrates. The mechanism used
for generating the hydrates is similar to the hydration process of Portland cement, and the more
calcium oxide the stabilizer contains, the quicker the pozzolanic reaction can be. The pozzolanic
reaction can be described as:
CaO + H2O −→Ca(OH)2
(2.1)
Ca(OH)2 + SiO2 −→C-S-H gel
(2.2)
Ca(OH)2 + Al2O3 −→C-A-H gel
(2.3)
13
==PAGE:21==
(Lin et al., 2007; Taki et al., 2020)
Another reason for soil improvement according to Lin et al. (2007), can be due to flocculation-
agglomeration (Lin et al., 2007), where the calcium oxide from the cement and hydrated lime
reacts with the clayey soil, as OH−ions enter the soil/water solution, which makes the soil particles
flocculate and change to a coarser structure (Dhir et al., 2017a).
One way to examine the indication of hydration and strength development when using SSA in
combination with lime or cement is to examine the pH (Dhir et al., 2017a). In alkaline conditions,
the pH will elevate, which makes the silica and alumina in the soil react with the Ca2+ ions from the
lime and form calcium silicate hydrates and aluminum silicate hydrates. Over time the hydrates
crystallize and bind the soil particles. Dhir et al. (2017a) show that when only SSA is applied, the
pH is not elevated, indicating that there is no significant reaction taking place between the soil and
the SSA. When SSA/lime or SSA/cement is added, the pH is increased. As the calcium ions are
used in the hydration reaction over time, the alkalinity (the pH) will dissipate. This indicates that
bond formation and strengthening of the soil are occurring (Dhir et al., 2017a).
The hydration products can also be examined by XRD, SEM, or TGA. Contrary to Dhir et al.
(2017a), Kadhim et al. (2022) observed C-S-H gel and CH from a SEM analysis of a soil mixed
with 20% SSA, indicating that the SSA had reacted with the soil.
2.2.2
SSA in research
SSA is still undergoing research to figure out how and where to utilize the SSA. So far, it has been
used as supplementary cementitious material in concrete, and as a substitution for fine aggregates
in ceramics and fired bricks or for soil stabilization of earth-based materials for geotechnical ap-
plications (Lynn et al., 2018; Aparna, 2022). But at the moment the variety of the SSA is very
high, thus the specific SSA must be tested and evaluated before application (Ottosen et al., 2015).
Both Kadhim et al. (2022) and Lin et al. (2007) examine SSA in relation with geotechnical appli-
cations focusing on soft cohesive subgrade soil, while Taki et al. (2020) also focus on geotechnical
applications but by using sewage sludge mixed with lime as a construction material. Lynn et al.
(2018) has a broader view and focus on the leaching behaviour of SSA used within different ap-
plications such as ceramics, concrete, road pavements, geotechnical applications, and lightweight
aggregates.
14
==PAGE:22==
Kadhim et al. (2022) tested the geotechnical properties of soft clayey soil mixed with 10%, 15%,
and 20% SSA by weight of dry soil. The specimens were tested after 3, 14, and 28 days of curing.
The conclusion was that SSA can improve the geotechnical properties of soft clayey soil, where
the swelling behaviour was reduced and the unconfined compressive strength, and triaxial shear
strength were enhanced. The optimum mix was 10% SSA, but all mixes with SSA reached higher
compressives strengths than untreated soil. Kadhim et al. (2022) highlights that by using SSA as a
stabilizer for soft clayey soil, it is possible to reduce the environmental risk, compared to disposing
SSA on landfills and open areas (Kadhim et al., 2022).
Lin et al. (2007) examined the geotechnical properties when using 0%, 2%, 4%, 8%, and 16% SSA
and hydrated lime as stabilizers for soft cohesive subgrade soil. The samples were cured for 3h, 3,
7, 14, 28, 56, and 90 days. Lin et al. (2007) concluded that SSA and hydrated lime improves the
geotechnical properties of the soft cohesive subgrade soil, as the unconfined compressive strength
was 3 to 7 times better than untreated soil and the swelling behaviour was also reduced effectively.
According to Lin et al. (2007) it is the pozzolanic reaction or flocculation-agglomeration from
the SSA and hydrated lime, that causes an increase of compaction, shear strength, and bearing
capacity. The soil with 16% SSA/hydrated lime reached the highest compressive strength, but 8%
of SSA/hydrated lime could effectively improve the swelling behaviour and change the soil from
a poor subgrade soil to a good subgrade soil.
Lynn et al. (2018) focused on the environmental considerations of using SSA within construction
materials, such as cement clinker, lighweight aggregates, mortar and concrete, SSA blocks, road
pavements, geotechnical applictaions and ceramics. The paper focus on the leaching behaviour
of the different materials when mixed with SSA. Lynn et al. (2018) found that SSA contains
potentially harmful heavy metals, but most samples could be classified as non-hazardous. Within
geotechnical applications it was found that the leaching was depending on the seepage rate, when
used as landfill liner material. When using SSA as a filler in Controlled Low-Strength Materials,
and combining it with blastfurnace slag, the leaching was below stringent soil quality standards. In
ceramics, the leaching of SSA-tiles was below drinking water parameters, because of the binding
effects of clay and high temperatures.
Taki et al. (2020) examine the potential use of lime-treated sewage sludge as a construction mate-
rial for subbase of flexible pavements and whether it can enhance geotechnical properties. Sewage
sludge samples with 0%, 2%, 4%, 6%, and 8% of lime by weight, were cured for 7, 14, and 28
15
==PAGE:23==
days. The highest compressive strength was achieved by 6% lime for all three curing conditions,
and the compressive strength increased with time. Increasing from 665 kPa after 7 days of cur-
ing to 1102 kPa after 28 days of curing. The compressive strength of samples with lime were all
greater than the one without lime. The study concludes that lime treating can be used to stabilize
sewage sludge, making it possible to use as a construction material for subbase of flexible pave-
ments. XRD, SEM, and TGA were performed, which confirmed formation of compounds, that had
improved the enginnering properties. Taki et al. (2020) suggest that lime-treated sewage sludge
can be used instead of sand or other granular fill in embankments, slope repair, and bearing stratum
for high load foundations and can help both sludge management and engineering applications.
Aparna (2022) made a review of different researches that uses SSA for soil stabilization. Studies
have shown that clay bricks with 10% SSA show higher compressive strength than standard clay
bricks.
The above researches show that studies regarding earth building materials and SSA mainly con-
sider SSA and soil improvement in relation to geotechnical applications. It has been difficult to
find research papers which examine SSA in relation to CEB. Research have been done on other
types of binders in relation with CEB, e.g. Boussaa et al. (2023) have tested CEB with the use of
woodbased ash, Lima et al. (2012) with the use of sugarcane bagasse ash, Elahi et al. (2020) with
saw dust ash and cement, and Malkanthi et al. (2020) have tested CEB using lime and cement to
stabilize the soil. The same has Narayanaswamy et al. (2020), who also included alkali activators.
Studies concerning unstabilized CEB was also found, e.g. Wiehle et al. (2022) who examined
ustabilized CEB in relation to relative humidity.
2.2.2.1
SSA influence on compressive strength
The main reason for utilizing SSA in earth construction is to enhance the shear and compresive
strength of soil, thus it is the compressive strength test that determines the optimal content of SSA
(Aparna, 2022). The compressive strength depends on the initial soil properties, curing time, con-
tent of ash added, water content and compaction time. Studies have shown that increasing curing
time and content of ash increases the strength. Some studies also show an optimum of the amount
of ash added, where an increase of ash decrease the strength (Aparna, 2022). Aparna (2022) gives
four explanations for the increase in strength when adding SSA: 1) The soil permeability is re-
duced because of a chemical reaction between the SSA and soil, which plugs the voids in the soil.
2) The SSA is shaped as angular glassy spheres that may increase the bonding between the soil
16
==PAGE:24==
grains and the SSA. 3) The calcium oxide from the SSA reacts with the silica and alumina of the
soil, which creates hydration products. 4) The cohesion and internal friction is enhanced due to the
cementing property of the SSA and its capacity to modify absorbed water films (Aparna, 2022).
The study by Kadhim et al. (2022) supports the findings by Aparna (2022). The soils with SSA
reached higher strengths than the unstabilized soil. The strength increased with the curing time,
and 10% of SSA was found as the optimum amount of SSA, as an increase in SSA reduced
the compressive strength with increasing curing time, except for the test with 20% SSA which
reached the highest compressive strength after 28 days of curing. Kadhim et al. (2022) reached a
compressive strength of 190 kPa when using 10% SSA both when cured at 3, 14 and 28 days.
The study by Lin et al. (2007) reached also a compressive strength of 190 kPa after 90 days of
curing, when using 16% SSA and hydrated lime. The 28 days compressive strength were about
130 kPa.
Aparna (2022) found that the optimum amount of SSA for soil stabilization ranges from 7-10% of
the dry weight of the soil.
2.2.2.2
SSA influence on Atterberg limits
Various studies have shown that the plasticity index and liquid limit is reduced when soil is stabi-
lized with SSA. The same accounts for soil stabilized with lime. A combination of SSA and lime
would reduce the plasticity index further. The reason for the reduction in plasticity is explained
by the pozzolanic reaction, cation exchange and agglomoration of clay particles, which produces
C-S-H. This decreases the water absorption, which increases the workability, and making the soil
more brittle (Aparna, 2022). This is supported by Kadhim et al. (2022) and Lin et al. (2007).
Kadhim et al. (2022) found that the liquid limit and plastic limit increased slightly after 3 days of
curing, because of the high water absorption affinity of the SSA. But after 28 days of curing, the
liquid limit and the plastic limit decreased with increasing content of SSA, due to the hydration
products. The study also showed that the soil became more brittle and lacked plastic behaviour
when more than 10% SSA was added to the soil. Lin et al. (2007) also found the plasticity index
to decrease when adding SSA and hydrated lime. Aparna (2022) do also mention that contradic-
tory results have been found, which showed that the plastic limit and liquid limit increased with
increasing content of SSA, due to the water absorption affinity.
17
==PAGE:25==
2.2.2.3
SSA influence on compaction and optimal moisture content
Kadhim et al. (2022) found that the dry density decreased with increasing SSA content, while the
OMC increased. Lin et al. (2007) found that utilizing SSA/hydrated lime as a soil stabilizer had
little effect in compacting the soil, as the OMC and dry density was the same for all contents of
SSA/hydrated lime. According to Aparna (2022) multiple studies found that the OMC increases
with increasing content of SSA, and that the dry density reduces. This is explained by the SSA
having a lower specific gravity than the soil, and that cemented products are instantly created,
which decreases the dry density. Studies have also shown the opposite, thus the optimal moisture
content decreases with increasing content of SSA, which increases the dry density. Samples with
this behaviour is more suitable for sub-grade materials. The reason for the decrease in OMC
is according to Aparna (2022) attributed to that the specific surface of SSA is smaller than the
soils, leading to a denser and better compaction, which increases the dry density. One study also
reported that when the moisture content increased, the soil particles got lubricated which increased
the workability and the packing of the soil particles (Aparna, 2022).
2.2.2.4
SSA in combination with quicklime
Research indicate that when combining SSA with hydrated lime, the soil and mechanical proper-
ties becomes even better than when only using SSA (Kadhim et al., 2022). The study by Lin et al.
(2007) indicate that the admixture of SSA/hydrated lime can stabilize soft subgrade soil. Taki et
al. (2020) found that the pH and electrical conductivity decreased after 28 days of curing, when
the sewage sludge was mixed with quicklime. Taki et al. (2020) attribute the decrease to cemen-
tation. The combination of sewage sludge and lime resulted in higher compressive strengths than
the sample without lime.
2.2.2.5
Leaching behavior
The utilization of SSA as a construction material, depends on whether there is a risk of leaching
heavy metals. The suitability of the soil at end-of-life is largely depending on the purity of the
material (Schroeder, 2016). In this context it is necessary to understand whether the use of SSA
can lead to any damage of the environment.
Dhir et al. (2017b) states that the rate at which harmful heavy metals are able to leach out of the
SSA is depending on its permeability. The permeability of SSA equals the permeability of "clean
sands and sand gravel mixtures" and "very fine sand, silts and clay-silt laminates" according to
18
==PAGE:26==
the british standard BS 8004. Since permeability is depending on the pore size, which is related
to the particle size, it is assumed that the grading of SSA can be compared with the grading of
soil categories. This may leave the material more susceptible to percolation-controlled leaching,
especially when used in unbound applications (Dhir et al., 2017b).
When SSA is used as a soil stabilizing agent in soft clayey soil, it is sometimes combined with
cement or hydrated lime. Research indicates that cement, lime, and cohesive soil particles can
restrict leaching of heavy metals from SSA (Dhir et al., 2017b). Taki et al. (2020) showed that
when sewage sludge is lime treated, it reduces the leaching of the metal ions from the sewage
sludge. Ottosen et al. (2015) found that when SSA is treated with electrodialytic separation
(EDS) to extract phosphorous from the SSA, some concentrations of heavy metals increased,
while other reduced. But it was expected that the heavy metals left in the EDS treated SSA were
very immobile. Ottosen et al. (2015) also state that the content of heavy metals in general are
limited and heavy metal such as Cu, Cr, and Zn were all below the limit values for using residual
products in agricultural purposes. When Ottosen et al. (2015) compared the heavy metals from
the SSA with the heavy metals from coal ash, which is currently used in concrete, the content of
Ni and Cr were higher in the coal ash than in the SSA.
The leaching behaviour also depends on the pH. The solubility of heavy metals is typically low
when the pH is neutral, while the risk of leaching increases with an increase of an alkaline or
acidic environment. The average pH of SSA is 9 (Dhir et al., 2017b).
At the moment there is a great variability in SSA, thus it is necessary to evaluate the material
before use. It is expected that the variability will decrease, when the material is accepted as a
construction material in the industry (Dhir et al., 2017b).
2.2.2.6
Expected benefits of using SSA in earth
Based on the litterature it is expected that the SSA will enhance the mechanical properties of
the soil by increasing the compressive strength and reducing shrinkage compared to unstabilized
soil. Research give contradictory results on whether the OMC and dry density is decreased or
increased by the addition of SSA. It is also expected that the performance is further enhanced
when combining SSA with lime.
Additionally, there is an environmental gain in terms of reducing land filling, energy consumption
and resource depletion if the SSA can be utilized within construction materials (Aparna, 2022).
19
==PAGE:27==

3.1
Characterization of soil
The soils listed in table 3.1 have been examined in the following sections. Additionally, RS-REF
from table 3.3 have been examined for linear shrinkage and Atterberg limits.
The following laboratory tests were conducted to characterize the soils.
• Loss on ignition
• Density of solid particles
• Determination of water content
• Particle size distribution
• Atterberg limits
• Linear shrinkage
• Clay mineralogy (X-ray Diffraction)
3.1.1
Loss on ignition
The purpose of determining the loss on ignition (LOI) was to find the amount of organic matter
present in the soils. The loss on ignition can describe whether the soils are suitable as an earth
construction material, as well as whether it is necessary to remove the organic matter before the
determination of particle size distribution.
Loss on ignition was performed in accordance with DS/EN 15935:2021.
The soils were dried at 105°C before test execution, and ground with a mortar. The test is very
sensible, and even fingerprints can influence the results. Therefore the crucibles were cleaned by
heating at 550°C for 30 minutes. The weight of the crucibles (𝑚𝑐) could be determined when they
were cooled down. 2.5 g of soil was added to each crucible, and three samples were produced for
each soil type. The soils were then heated at 550°C for one hour to determine the organic matter.
In addition to the standard, the soils were heated at 950°C for one hour, to determine the content
of calcite in the soils. The crucibles were weighed after each heating to four significant digits, and
the difference in weight described the loss on ignition.
22
==PAGE:30==
Figure 3.2: Samples after being in the muffle oven at 550°C (left), and 950°C (center). The muffle oven is
shown in the figure to the right.
The loss on ignition in percentage can be calculated by formula 3.1.
𝑤𝐿𝑂𝐼= 𝑚𝑐−𝑚𝑑

3.1.2
Density of solid particles
The particle density of the soils was determined by the pycnometer method. The pycnometer
method is based on the fluid displacement method, by determining the volume of the soil. The
density is calculated by knowing the mass and the volume of the soil (DS/CEN ISO/TS 17892-
3:2004).
23
==PAGE:31==
The test was performed in accordance with DS/CEN ISO/TS 17892-3:2004, except that no water
bath was used. The temperature was kept constant in the room, and the temperature of the control
liquid was taken before and after the air had been removed. The test was performed on natural soil
and the dry mass of the soil was determined by taking a sample for water determination.
Before test execution, the soils were sieved through a 4mm sieve, to separate the soils into a
granular material. 75 g of soil was divided into three 100 ml pycnometers. The average of the
samples described the particle density of the soils.
Figure 3.3: 100ml pycnometers filled with control liquid and soil
The particle density can be calculated by formula 3.3.

3.1.4
Particle size distribution
The particle size distribution was performed in accordance with DS/CEN ISO/TS 17892-4:2004.
The complete curve for grain size distribution was created by conducting both sieving and sedi-
mentation tests using a hydrometer. Organic content and carbonate content were not removed as
the soils could be defined as low-organic soil, since the content of organic matter was less than 2%
to 6% of dry mass (DS/EN ISO 14688-2:2018, table 3). The loss on ignition from section 3.1.1
was determined to be 2.8%, which also included the loss of mass due to the loss of clay minerals
when heated at 550°C.
3.1.4.1
Sieving
The sieving was executed on an amount of approximately 500 g of dry soil, which was wet sieved
through a 63µm sieve. The soil had before sieving been dried at 50°C for four days and soaked in a
dispersing agent of 0.007M Natrium pyrophosphate to remove interstitial clay. The mass retained
on the sieve was dried, and the mass which passed was thrown away to make the wet sieving
process faster and easier to execute.
25
==PAGE:33==
Figure 3.4: Wetsieving through 63µm sieve (left). Soil >63µm (right).
The dried soil >63µm, was sieved through a sieving tower consisting of 4mm, 2mm, 1mm, 0.5mm,
0.25mm, 0.125mm, and 0.063mm sieves and a pan. The sieving was performed on a shaking table
for a minimum of 10 minutes. The mass retained on each sieve was weighed by weighing the
sieve before and after the shaking table. The mass retained was calculated as the mass of the sieve
without soil subtracted from the mass of the sieve with soil.
Figure 3.5: Sieving tower on shaking table

3.1.5
Atterberg limits
The Atterberg limit tests were performed to be able to describe the critical states of the soil, which
can be described by the liquid limit and the plastic limit. The liquid limit describes the moisture
content at which the soil passes from a liquid state to a plastic state, whereas the plastic limit
describes the moisture content, at which the soil is too dry to be plastic and goes from a ductile
behavior to a brittle behavior (Aparna, 2022; Fabbri et al., 2022).
29
==PAGE:37==
The Atterberg limits were determined according to DS/CEN ISO/TS 17892-12:2004 by using the
fall-cone method for the liquid limit and the cord test for the plastic limit.
3.1.5.1
Fall-cone method
The fall-cone method was executed with the 60g/60°fall-cone which requires an initial penetration
of about 7 mm, and four measurements within the range of 7mm to 15mm.
Figure 3.8: Falling cone
The soil was prepared by soaking approximately 500 g of dry material in distilled water, and wet
sieving it through a 2mm sieve and a 500µm sieve. The soil retained was dried at 50 °C.
30
==PAGE:38==
Figure 3.9: Wet sieving (left) and excess water after centrifugation of the yellow clay, Y (right, top), and
the red clay, R (right, bottom)
During the wet sieving process too much water was added, thus the soil samples passing the sieve
were centrifuged at 3000 rpm for 25-30 minutes to remove the excess water. The excess water for
the yellow clays (Y, YS) was very clear, while the excess water from the red clays (R, RS) was
less clear. This indicate that a minimal fraction of <2µm was lost for the yellow clays, and some
of the fraction <2µm was lost for the red clays during the pre-treating process. This may have
influenced the final results. After removing the excess water, the soil samples were left to dry at
room temperature overnight to get the correct consistency for the fall-cone method.
When testing, each measurement of the falling cone, was done twice for double determination of
the liquid limit at different water contents. About 20 g was taken from the final measurement of
the double determination and placed in an oven at 105 °C to determine the corresponding water
content of the measured penetration.
The liquid limit corresponds to a cone penetration of 10mm.
3.1.5.2
Plasticity (cord test)
The plasticity test was performed on approximately 20 g of soil from the first or second fall-cone
measurement, to ensure a consistency that was able to form into a ball by the palms of the hands,
after the fall-cone test.
The ball was divided into two subsections to make a double determination. Each was further
divided into four, where each was rolled to a cord of 3mm until it crumbled. When the cord
31
==PAGE:39==
crumbled, the plastic limit had been reached. The crumbles were put into the oven at 105°C, to
determine the water content at the plastic limit.
Figure 3.10: The different steps in testing the plasticity.
The plasticity index, 𝐼𝑝, can be calculated by subtracting the plastic limit from the liquid limit.
𝐼𝑝= 𝑤𝐿−𝑤𝑝
(3.9)
where,
𝑤𝐿
is the liquid limit [%]
𝑤𝑝
is the plastic limit [%]
3.1.6
Linear shrinkage
The linear shrinkage was determined to quantify how much the different soil types shrink. The
test performed was based on the British Standard BS 1377-2:1990, section 6.5.
For the test execution, the soil sample must have passed a 425µm sieve, and have a moisture
content about the liquid limit of the soil (BS 3177-2:1990, section 6.5.4.1-2).
A small sample was collected from the soil at the falling cone test when the soil reached the liquid
limit (10 mm penetration). The soil was put in a brass mould with a palette knife, and air pockets
were tried to be removed when applying the soil in the mould.
The moulds with the soil samples were set aside in a fume cupboard for a minimum of four
days for them to dry at room temperature. To reduce the risk of cracks by too fast drying, the
moulds with the samples were weighed twice, with a minimum of 1h apart, to ensure that the
samples had stabilized, before moving the moulds to a higher temperature. After drying at room
temperature, the moulds were transferred to a 60°C oven, and after stabilizing at 60°C, the samples
32
==PAGE:40==
were transferred to a 105°C oven to finish drying. When the drying had stabilized at 105°C, the
length of the samples was measured.
Figure 3.11: Samples before drying (left). Samples after drying at 105°C (right).
The linear shrinkage of the soil was calculated as a percentage of the original length of the speci-
men, by using formula 3.10.
Percentage of linear shrinkage = (1 −𝐿𝐷
𝐿0
) · 100
(3.10)
where,
𝐿𝐷
is the length of the oven-dried specimen [mm]
𝐿0
is the original length [mm]
3.1.7
Clay mineralogy
X-Ray diffraction (XRD) can help in analyzing which clay minerals are present in the loams. By
identifying the clay minerals, it can indicate whether the loam may tend to swell or not (Brindley,
1981).
X-Ray diffraction was performed on the soil samples without sand, Y and R. The mineral content
was assumed to be the same for the soils with sand, YS and RS, as the soils are similar to Y, and
R, just with 15% and 6% sand added, respectively.
The XRD was executed on glass plates with soil < 2µm where the content of CaCO3 had been
33
==PAGE:41==
removed. The production of glass plates had been produced by following the guide by Schnell
(2016) which was provided by the laboratory technicians at DTU.
Briefly described, the following steps were performed to prepare the soil for the glass plates.
About 200 g of soil was dried at 40°C and ground with a mortar. The soil was mixed with acetic
acid to remove CaCO3. When it stopped bubbling, the acetic acid was washed away by using a
centrifuge at 3000rpm for 10 min. It was washed until it stopped smelling of acetic acid. The
clays were then dried at 60°C for three days. The soil was ground with a mortar to homogenize
the soil and 20 g was weighed and put into a plastic bottle. The 20 g soil was mixed with 0.002M
Na-pyro-phosphate and placed in a machine that rotated the samples for 18h. The samples were
then placed in ultrasound for 15 min. After the ultrasound, the samples were wet sieved through
a 63µm sieve by using 0.002M Na-pyro-phosphate. After wet sieving, the samples were poured
into Andreasen’s cylinders to rest for 16h and 30 min. The cylinders were emptied so only the
fraction <2µm was retained in the plastic bottles. The plastic bottles were centrifuged and excess
water was thrown out. Left was a clay paste that was set to dry at room temperature in a fume
cupboard. The dried clay paste was then grounded very fine, and 0.03 g of soil was mixed with
1.5 ml distilled water. The mixtures were applied to four glass plates, two for each soil type. Only
one glass plate was analyzed by XRD, the other one was made in case the first one was damaged
during testing.
Figure 3.12: Andreasen’s cylinders with fraction <2µm (left), glass plates (center), X-ray diffractometer
(right)
The XRD measurements were performed at four different conditions. The glass plates were
1. saturated with water
34
==PAGE:42==
2. saturated with ethylene glycol for a minimum of 2 days at 60°C
3. ignited at 350°C for 2 hours
4. ignited at 550°C for 2 hours
Before each measurement, the samples were cooled down at room temperature.
The measurements were conducted by using the following programs:
Water saturated sample: 3-65°, 21min, 15mm, 2°, 8.7.
Ethylene glycolate sample: 3-40°, 13min, 10mm, 2°.
Ignited samples: 3-40°, 13 min, 10mm, 2°.
The different pre-treatments and measurements helped analyze the mineral content of the clays,
as XRD measures the d-spacing and intensity of the clay minerals in the range of 2-40°. The
minerals can then be identified based on their reflections under the different conditions (Brindley,
1981; Lugwisha, 2011).
3.2
Characterization of SSA
The sewage sludge ash (SSA) selected for this project was #0013 Avedøre-Krüger-4 from Amager
forbrænding. The SSA was milled at 1000rpm for 10 sec to increase the reactivity of the ash
(Ottosen et al., 2015). No metals or nutrients were removed from the ash.
Figure 3.13: SSA milled by a vibrating cup mill at 1000rpm for 10 sec
35
==PAGE:43==
The following laboratory tests were conducted to characterize the SSA.
• Particle size distribution
• Leaching, pH, and conductivity
• Digestion
3.2.1
Particle size distribution
The particle size distribution of the SSA and Qz was done by laser diffractometer, using the wet
method with ethanol. The ethanol makes the curves smoother by limiting the agglomeration of
particles. The particle size distribution was conducted with the help of the laboratory technicians.
Before testing, the ash was dried at 105°C. When performing the particle size distribution, the
particles should be smaller than 250µm, thus the ash is usually sieved through a 250µm sieve
before testing. Since this ash was milled at 1000rpm for 10 sec, it was concluded that the particle
size would be small enough to skip the sieving process.
0.5 g of ash was mixed with 18 ml ethanol in a 20 ml plastic vial. The mixture of ethanol and
ash was added to the laser diffractometer until the laser obscuration reached 16%. For each test
sample, three measurements were made, and the average was calculated.
3.2.2
Leaching, pH and conductivity
Leaching was performed on the SSA, to figure out if there could be an environmental risk of
using the material in construction materials. The pH and conductivity can give an indication of
how easily heavy metal leaches, while the leaching test states how much there is leached. The
tests were conducted by supervision of the laboratory technicians at DTU and were performed in
accordance with the guide by Ottosen (2023).
Before testing, the ash was heated to 105°C for a minimum of 24h. Three samples were prepared.
10 g of ash and 100 ml of distilled water were added to a 250 ml plastic container. The samples
were placed on a shaking table for 24h. After shaking for 24h, the samples rested for 15 minutes.
After resting the liquid was filtered through a Whatman 42 filter paper with 0.45µm pore size, using
a vacuum pump, and 15 ml were transferred to a plastic vial. Afterward, the pH and conductivity
were measured on the three samples. The content of heavy metals were measured on the IC and
ICP.
36
==PAGE:44==
Figure 3.14: Preparation of plastic vials for IC and ICP (left). Electrodes to measure the pH and conduc-
tivity (right).
3.2.3
Digestion
Digestion of the SSA was examined to find the total amount of heavy metals present in the ash.
The test was performed by supervision of the laboratory technicians at DTU and followed the
American standard EPA3051A. Three samples were produced. Before testing, the milled SSA
was heated at 105°C for a minimum of 24h. 0.5 g was measured and placed in a centrifuge tube.
10 ml of nitric acid was added. The test samples were placed in the Microwave Digestion System,
Multiwave GO, for 20 min. Afterward, they were cooled for 10 min at room temperature. After
heating in the microwave, the test samples were vacuum filtered through a 0.45µm filter. The acid
mixture was diluted by adding distilled water up to 50 ml mark in a volumetric flask. After diluting
the mixture, 20 ml was transferred to a plastic vial. The amount of heavy metals was measured on
the IC and ICP.
The unit used for digestion is mg/kg dry matter. The ICP listed the heavy metals in mg/l, thus
formula 3.11 was used to change the unit from mg/l to mg/kg dry matter.
𝑚𝑔/𝑘𝑔=
𝐴· 0.100𝐿· 𝐶
𝐵· 10−3𝑘𝑔/𝑔𝑎𝑠ℎ
(3.11)
where,
A
is the concentration of heavy metal [mg/l]
B
is the ash weighed [g]
C
is the dilution factor [-]
37
==PAGE:45==
3.3
Production and testing of specimens
To test the mechanical and physical properties of the different soil mixes, cylinder specimens of
Ø60mm and H60mm, were produced by compacting the specimens in cylinder steel moulds using
an EME-052-instron-6022 compression machine. The same machine was used for de-moulding
the specimens.
The following specimens were produced for the different tests:
• 12 specimens of raw soil (RS) to test the production method.
• 32 specimens (min 5 of each soil mix) to test the optimal moisture content (OMC).
• 65 specimens (13 of each soil mix) to test the compression strength, leaching, pH, and
sorption capacity.
3.3.1
Pre-tests
Pre-tests were made before producing the final test specimens to find a suitable production method.
It was examined whether 1) a pre-treatment of the soil was needed, 2) at what pressure the speci-
mens could be produced, and 3) at what moisture content the specimens should have by examining
the OMC.
Houben & Guillaud (1994) described the necessity of grinding/pulverizing and screening the soil
before mixing. This was not done, because the soil was ordered as a commercial product in-
tended for the production of fired bricks, thus it was expected that it already had undergone a
pre-treatment.
3.3.1.1
Production method
Samples were compacted at different setups to find the optimal pressure and production method
before producing the final test specimens.
The first series was created with the red clay with sand (RS) at its natural state (RS-RAW) to see
if it was possible to get nicely compacted cylinders with limited air gaps without pre-treating the
soil. About 350 g of soil was added to an oiled steel cylinder with a diameter of Ø60mm and a
height of 120mm.
The following compression setups were tested:
38
==PAGE:46==
1. 6 kN, 50 mm/min, absolute ramp
2. 8 kN, 50 mm/min, absolute ramp
3. 8 kN, 50 mm/min, cyclic 3.5
4. 4 kN, 50 mm/min, absolute ramp
5. 9.5 kN, 50 mm/min, absolute ramp
The pressure added was in the range of 1-4 MPa, which according to Houben & Guillaud (1994)
corresponded to a very low or low pressure.
Table 3.4: Moulding pressure

3.3.1.2
Optimal moisture content
The optimal moisture content was examined for six different soil mixes: RS-RAW, RS-REF, RS-
SSA, RS-CaO, RS-CaO-SSA, and RS-CaO-Qz. The OMC was found by producing test specimens
with a minimum of five different water contents by using the EME-052-Instron-6022, which also
were used for the final production of test specimens. 350g ± 1g of soil was used for each specimen,
39
==PAGE:47==
and two specimens were produced at each water content to calculate an average. The soil was
mixed with water by hand in a plastic container and sealed overnight. The different soil mixes
were tested in the range of 12-22% water, depending on how the soil mixture behaved. At first,
three mixes with different water contents were produced, and afterward, it was figured out whether
the water content should be increased or decreased to find the optimum. After the production of
specimens at each water content, the actual water content was calculated by drying 3x20 g of soil
mix at 105°C for a minimum of 24h. This water content is later referred to as the "true" water
content.
The bulk and dry density were calculated by formula 3.13 and 3.14 from the Proctor method
(DS/EN 13286-2:2011).
𝜌= 𝑚

3.3.2
Production of final test specimens
The pre-examinations of the production method and water content resulted in the following pro-
duction method for the final test specimens.
Figure 3.15: Production method of test specimens
40
==PAGE:48==
Five different soil mixes were used to produce the test specimens, see table 3.3. The water content
was based on the soil mixes OMC, except for RS-REF, due to a calculation error.
The binder content [wt%] was calculated based on the dry soil weight. The water content [wt%]
was calculated based on the total dry weight, thus the weight of soil and binder. The specific mix
recipes used for the production of test specimens can be seen in appendix C, table C.2.
Figure 3.15 illustrate the production method.
First, the soil was broken down by hand and dried at 50°C until equilibrium. This took approxi-
mately 3 days. The soil and sand were then added to a container with a lid, and water correspond-
ing to the OMC was poured over the soil. The soil, sand, and water were left to rest at night with
the lid on. This step made mixing easier, as the soil had time to redistribute the water. It was
experienced that a minimum amount of water was evaporated.
Figure 3.16: Soil mixer, MADO Cutter MKT 661 (left). Compression machine, EME-52-Instron-6022
(right)
The soil, sand, and water were then mixed with the binder in the MADO Cutter MKT 661 for
10 minutes to get a homogeneous soil mix. Approximately 350 g of the soil mix was added to
an oiled cylindrical steel mould with a diameter of Ø60, and the soil was compressed using the
EME-052-Instron-6022 machine, compressing at 8 kN with a displacement-controlled speed of
100mm/min. The height of the specimens was 60mm ± 2mm, which resulted in an aspect ratio of
1. After producing the test specimens, a sample of the soil of 20 g was placed in a 105°C oven,
to measure the water content. The test specimens were wrapped in plastic to prevent water from
41
==PAGE:49==
evaporating and put into a plastic bag, which was sealed.
Figure 3.17: Moist RS-SSA specimens (top). Specimens wrapped in plastic (bottom).
8 specimens were placed in the climate chamber at 23°C, and 5 of the specimens were furthermore
put in a foil bag, and placed in a water bath at 40°C, for curing in 14 days. Since the bags could
not hold tight, the specimens were transferred to an oven at 40°C, and sealed in a new plastic and
foil bag. After curing the plastic was removed from the specimens, and left to dry in a climate
chamber of 23°C, and 65%RH for 7 days. After drying the specimens were ready for testing.
Compression tests were performed on specimens from both curing conditions. pH, leaching, and
sorption isotherms were performed on specimens that had cured at 23°C.
Figure 3.18: Dry specimens. From the left: RS-REF, RS-SSA, RS-CaO, RS-CaO-SSA, RS-CaO-Qz.
42
==PAGE:50==
After compression the specimens were weighed and the height and the bulk and dry density were
calculated by formula 3.13 and 3.14 presented in section 3.3.1.2
3.3.3
Compression test
The compression test was executed in accordance with DS/EN ISO 17892-7:2018, except that the
specimens were having an aspect ratio of 1:1 instead of 2:1 which is standard when executing the
unconfined compression test on cylindrical specimens. The EME-051-instron-6025 was used to
compress the test specimens at a displacement-controlled rate of 0.5mm/min. The displacement
and force applied were monitored, resulting in a force-displacement curve. Before compressing
the specimen, the mass and height of the specimen were measured to calculate the bulk and dry
density. The specimen was placed between a fixed steel plate and a plate on a ball joint, which
could move to correct an uneven surface. The compression test was stopped manually when cracks
started to develop and the force applied had dropped. Five specimens of each soil mix and curing
condition were tested. The water content was measured by drying one specimen from each soil
mix at 105 °C after failure.
Figure 3.19: Test setup for the unconfined compression test.
43

3.3.4
Leaching
Leaching was performed on test specimens containing SSA, which was cured at 23°C, to see the
risk of leaching heavy metals when the soil was mixed with the SSA. The procedure was similar
to the method for the ash in section 3.2.2.
The test specimens were crushed with a mortar and dried at 105°C for 24h. 10 g were mixed with
100 ml distilled water in a 250 ml plastic bottle. Three samples were made and placed on a shaking
table for 24h. After shaking, the samples rested for 15 minutes. The liquid was afterward filtered
through a Whatman 42 filter paper with 0.45µm pore size, using a vacuum pump, and 15 ml were
44
==PAGE:52==
transferred to a plastic vial. The pH and conductivity were measured by a pH and conductivity
electrode. The content of heavy metals was determined by using IC and ICP.
3.3.5
pH
The pH was measured on the test specimens cured at 23°C, to see if the pH could explain why
mold was growing on the test specimens RS-REF and RS-SSA.
One test specimen cured at 23°C, of each soil mix, was crushed by using a mortar and sieved
through a 500µm sieve. 20 g of the crushed soil was mixed in a 250 ml plastic bottle with 100
ml distilled water without CO2, by using a volumetric pipette. Three samples were made. The
samples were placed on a shaking table for one hour. After shaking, the pH was measured directly
from the 250 ml plastic bottles using a pH electrode.
Figure 3.20: Measuring the pH of specimens cured at 23°C.
3.3.6
Hygroscopic sorption properties
The hygroscopic sorption properties were tested to examine how the different soil mixes performed
compared to similar and other conventional materials.
The hygroscopic sorption properties were determined by using the climatic chamber method from
DS/ISO 12571:2021. The test deviated from the standard by only using three relative humidities
between 30-95% instead of four. The number of different relative humidities was reduced due to
limited time, thus by using three relative humidities there was time to examine both sorption and
45
==PAGE:53==
desorption to see if the hysteresis phenomenon occurred.
The following three relative humidities were used: 33%, 75%, and 94%, as these are similar to
the ones able to maintain when using the desiccator method, thus it might be easier to relate the
results.
Test specimens cured at 23°C were crushed by a mortar into smaller pieces and sieved through a
4 mm sieve. By crushing the samples, the surface area was increased and the samples should be
faster at reaching equilibrium at each relative humidity. The soils were dried at 50°C for 48h until
the mass had reached equilibrium, to ensure that the specimens were dry before the beginning of
the experiment. 20 g ± 1g of soil was put in a petri dish and placed in the chamber. Three samples
for each soil mix were tested, and the average was calculated.
Figure 3.21: The samples tested. From the top: RS-REF, RS-SSA, RS-CaO, RS-CaO-SSA, RS-CaO-Qz
(left). Climate chamber used for testing the sorption capacity of the soil mixes (right).
The specimens were weighed every day until the mass change had reached equilibrium. The
mass must be constant through three measurements with a minimum of 24h apart, that do not
deviate with more than 0.1%. During weighing the climatic chamber was stopped for the fan
not to interfere with the balance, thus it was necessary to shortly open the climate chamber after
weighing to start the fan again. The temperature and relative humidity were monitored by using
an EasyLog data logger.
46
==PAGE:54==

4.1
Characterization of soil
4.1.1
Loss on ignition, density of solid particles, and water content
The results from the loss on ignition, density of solid particles, and water content are collected in
table 4.1.

4.1.1.1
Loss on ignition
For all four soil types, the loss on ignition at 550°C was about 2.7-2.8%. The largest loss on
ignition was found for the yellow clay without sand (Y) and the red clay with sand (RS). The loss
on ignition at 950°C was largest for the yellow clays, (Y, YS), with a loss on ignition of 11% and
9%, respectively, while the loss on ignition for the red clays, (R, RS), both were about 4%.
The organic matter should be less than 2% to 6% according to DS/EN ISO 14688-2:2018, table
3, to be considered low-organic soil. According to Houben & Guillaud (1994), the organic matter
should not exceed 2% to 4%, to be sure that it does not influence the characteristics of the soil. The
content of organic matter is defined by the loss on ignition at 550°C, which for all soil types was
at the lower end of the allowed amount of organic matter. In addition, does the 2.8% also account
for some clay minerals which disappear when heated at 550°C. Table 4.5 in section 4.1.5 presents
which clay minerals disappear at high temperatures. Based on these reflections, the organic matter
48
==PAGE:56==
was not removed before executing the consecutive tests.
The loss on ignition at 950°C describes the total mass loss including organic matter, clay minerals,
and carbonate (Fabbri et al., 2022). This corresponds with what was expected, as the loss on
ignition at 950°C was greatest for the yellow clays (Y, YS), which color depends on the content of
calcite (Andersen et al., 1989).
4.1.1.2
Density of solid particles
The particle density was found to be roughly the same for all soil types, although the particle
density was found to be greater for the soils with sand compared to the ones without sand.
The particle density used in the old standard for particle size distribution was normally 2.65 Mg/m3
(DS 405.8, 1978). Clayey and silty soils have a particle density between 2.6 and 2.9 (Das, 2007),
which the results fell within.
The results are used for the determination of the particle size distribution in section 4.1.2.
The measurements can be seen in appendix A.2.
4.1.1.3
Water content
The water content was greatest in the red clay with sand (RS), with a water content of 26.2%. The
lowest water content was found in the soil types without sand (Y, R). The standard deviation was
greatest for the red clays (R, RS).
The water content was also determined in combination with other tests, thus these results were
mainly used to get an insight into the material and worked as a reference.
4.1.2
Particle size distribution
The sieving and sedimentation test’s particle size distribution was plotted on the same semi-
logarithmic plot, shown in figure 4.1.
49
==PAGE:57==
Figure 4.1: Particle size distribution with recommendation for compressed earth blocks
There was a discontinuity between the sieving and the sedimentation curve. The two curves were
joined together to achieve a continuous, smooth curve, dictated by the sieving. The particle size
distribution for the four soil types was plotted together with the recommended zone from Houben
& Guillaud (1994). The zone suggests where the particle size distribution should lay, for the soils
to be suitable for the production of CEB. The zones provided by Houben & Guillaud (1994) are
not rigid, thus the soils can be suitable even though they lie outside the zone, but the soils are more
likely to be suitable if they fall within the zone (Houben & Guillaud, 1994).
The particle size distribution curves show that all four soil types contain about 20% clay. The
particle size distribution curves for the yellow clays, (Y, YS), deviate more from each other, than
the red clays (R, RS). This corresponds with the fact that the yellow clay with sand, YS, is the
yellow clay, Y, with 15% sand added, while the red clay with sand, RS, is the red clay, R, with
only 6% sand added, thus the red clays are more alike.
When looking at the curves they are relatively smooth, except for some discontinuities. At the
yellow clay, Y, two points lie next to each other near the 60% particles passing. This is because
50
==PAGE:58==
two measurements by the hydrometer were taken too quickly, not letting the sediment settle. For
the red clay, R, there is a point that drops in percentage near the 40% passing, which is due to a
wrong reading of the reference solution. For the red clay with sand, RS, there is a relatively large
and steep discontinuity between the sieving curve and the sedimentation curve. The joint is not as
smooth as the curves from the other soil types, making it slightly less well-graded.
From figure 4.1 it is noticed that all four soil types fall outside of the recommended zone by
Houben & Guillaud (1994). The red clay with sand, RS, is the one closest to the recommended
zone, thus it was examined whether the soil would become more suitable if it was modified with
sand. In figure 4.2 the red clay with sand, RS, was theoretically modified with 15% 0-2mm and
15% 0-4mm extra sand, respectively. A counterpoint for this action could be that the amount of
clay (binder) would be reduced. When looking at figure 4.2 the clay content seems to be reduced
from 20% to about 15%.
Figure 4.2: Modified particle size distribution with recommendation for compressed earth blocks
In the figure above, it can be seen that the curves lie better within the recommended zone when
51
==PAGE:59==
modified. The coarser particle sizes still fall outside of the recommended zone, and the curve is
somewhat steep, thus it would not help adding more sand, but coarser material is needed, although
coarse aggregates are nor wanted. It would most likely be better to mix the soil with another soil
that can counterbalance the content of fines (Houben & Guillaud, 1994).
4.1.3
Atterberg limits
The measurements for cone penetration and the calculation of the water content corresponding
to each measurement can be seen in the appendix in table A.24 and A.26. No post-sinking was
observed on any of the clays. The results are shown in figure 4.3, where the cone penetration is
plotted on a logarithmic scale.
Figure 4.3: Liquid limit
The liquid limit is determined as the water content at a cone penetration of 10mm. This was
calculated based on the regression lines from the legend in figure 4.3, where y = liquid limit [%]
and x = cone penetration [mm].
52
==PAGE:60==
The Atterberg limits for the four soil types, and the modified version of the red clay with sand, are
listed in table 4.2.

When looking at the Atterberg limits, the four soil types, as well as the modified soil, can be clas-
sified as fat clays, as the plasticity indexes are between 10-25% and the liquid limits are between
30-50% (Byggeriets Udviklingsråd, 1993). The plasticity index describes the range of moisture
contents over which the soil remains plastic (Dhir et. al., 2017a) and can indicate how workable
the soil is. A low plasticity index usually results in better workability in engineering applications
according to Lin et al. (2007). The results showed that the modified version, RS-mod, had the
lowest plasticity index.
Based on the liquid limit and the plasticity index, the soil type of the clays is determined by
using the plasticity chart from DS/EN ISO 14688-2:2018, figure 1. The plasticity chart has been
replicated by the use of the key corresponding to figure 1 in DS/EN ISO 14688-2:2018.
53
==PAGE:61==
Figure 4.4: Plasticity chart
The soil types can be classified as Cl = Clay, Si = Silt, and
plasticity as L = low, M = medium, H = high, V = very high, O = organic. The U-line and A-line
are described by the following equations:
U-line:
𝐼𝑝= 0.9(𝑤𝐿−8)
(4.1)
A-line:
𝐼𝑝= 0.73(𝑤𝐿−20)
(4.2)
According to the plasticity chart in figure 4.4 all four clays and the modified version of the red
clay with sand can be classified as a clay loam of medium plasticity.
54
==PAGE:62==
Figure 4.5: Plasticity chart - CEB
When comparing the results from the Atterberg limits (the plasticity index and the liquid limit)
with the plasticity chart for CEB from Houben & Guillaud (1994) in figure 4.5, all four clays and
the modified soil fitted into the hatched area. This indicated that the soils were suitable for the
production of unfired clay bricks, such as CEB.

During testing, it was prominent that the greatest shrinkage occurred during the first day at room
temperature. No cracks in the specimens were observed, but some of the specimens ended with a
curve, thus the length was measured on the top and the bottom of the specimen, and the average
was calculated. The complete measurements can be seen in appendix A.6.
When comparing the clays without sand, Y and R, the latter had the greatest percentage of shrink-
age. Furthermore, the clays with sand (YS, RS), had a smaller percentage of shrinkage than the
ones without sand (Y, R). The linear shrinkage of the modified soil (RS-mod) was similar to the
natural red clay with sand (RS). The modified soil was expected to shrink less than the red clay
with sand since the content of sand was increased. But since only the fraction <500µm was used
for the linear shrinkage test, most of the sand may have been washed away. The lack of difference
between the original red clay with sand, and the modified version may be also attributed to the fact
that the linear shrinkage was based on one sample and not the average of three.
The British Standard (BS 1377-2:1990) states that the soil used for linear shrinkage should be at
the liquid limit. The results can therefore be influenced if deviating from the liquid limit. The cone
penetration used for the different soil types was as follows: Y = 11mm, YS = 10mm, R = 9.5mm,
RS = 10mm, RS - mod = 10mm. The difference between the yellow and red clay without sand
may therefore be too large because the yellow clay was used when the water content was above
the liquid limit and the red clay when it was below the liquid limit. Additionally, the shrinkage
of the yellow clay may also be too large compared to the other soil types, due to the difference
in measuring the linear shrinkage at 11mm and 10mm cone penetration. Despite this error, the
results are still thought of indicating that the yellow clays tend to shrink less than the red clays.
56
==PAGE:64==

Figure 4.6: XRD of clay minerals
The following clay minerals was found to be present in the two loams:
Table 4.6: Clay minerals identified

7.2Å, 3.7Å
