Kaolinite
7.2Å, 3.7Å
The clay minerals were identified by comparing the results from the different treatments.
Smectite was identified as the air-dried peak shifted from 14Å to 16-17Å when the sample was
treated with ethylene glycol, and the peak collapsed when heated to 350°C and 550°C.
Illite was identified, since there was a peak at 10Å, and the peak was present throughout all treat-
58
==PAGE:66==
ments.
Kaolinite and chlorite were identified since there was a peak of about 7Å and 3.5Å, which col-
lapsed when heated. Kaolinite and chlorite can be distinguished when looking at the peaks when
heated. If the peak collapses when heated at 350°C, the clay mineral can be identified as chlorite.
If the peak only disappears at 550°C the clay mineral can be identified as kaolinite. In the figure
it was noticed that the peak only collapsed when heated at 550°C at 7Å and 3.7Å, thus it was
identified as kaolinite instead of chlorite.
At 14Å there could be a peak within the air-dried peak, which could indicate chlorite, as the peak
collapsed both at 350°C and 550°C.
The clay mineral content seemed to be the same for the two soil types, as the intensity was about
the same. The only difference was the intensity of quartz, which was higher in the red clay, R
compared to the yellow clay, Y. From the XRD it looked like the clays contained smectites, which
could be montmorillonite or vermiculites, which tend to swell. Swelling/shrinking is an undesired
behavior in earthen construction materials, as it can cause cracks due to a volume change. To be
more certain about the clay mineral content, it is usually a good idea to support the analysis with
e.g. an infrared analysis (Lugwisha, 2011). This has not been executed in this thesis.
4.2
Characterization of SSA

The average of the particle size distribution of the SSA is plotted on a semi-logarithmic plot in
figure 4.7 together with the particle size distribution from the quartz, Qz. The particle size dis-

obscuration used for the results was 16%, as the deviation between the three measurements was
smaller than when using a laser obscuration of 10%. The results used to produce the graph are
listed in appendix B. The average standard deviation of the SSA and Qz was 0.04 and 0.03, re-
spectively.

sponded to silts and fine sand. The particle sizes from the Qz were distributed from 2µm to 158µm.

4.2.2.1
pH and conductivity
The pH of the SSA was found to be slightly alkaline with a pH of 9, which matches the average pH
of 9 for SSA across research (Dhir et al., 2017b). The standard deviation of the pH was 0.1. The
conductivity was found to be 1.57 mS/cm, with a standard deviation of 0.03. A high or low pH
would indicate that the heavy metals easily leaches (Dhir et al., 2017b). Since the pH is relatively
close to neutral, it is not expected that the SSA easily leaches heavy metals.
4.2.2.2
Leaching
All results from the IC and ICP are below the minimum standard value for the standard curve,
except for sulfate, which is above the maximum standard value, hence the IC and ICP was not
61
==PAGE:69==
able to detect the amount of heavy metals leached from the SSA. The results did though indicate
that leaching of Cd, Cr, Cu, P, Pb, Zn, Chloride and Nitrate was limited. Since the content of
sulfate (953 mg/l) was above standard value, it is expected that the sulfate exceeds the limit value
from the residual product act, BEK nr 1672. When the limit value is exceeded the SSA can only
be used for purposes within category 3, which covers paved roads with max 10% seepage rate,
paved pathways with solid surfaces such as asphalt, concrete or flags, and foundations and floors
as long as it does not cause any problems with the indoor climate (Restproduktbekendtgørelsen,
2016).
4.2.2.3
Digestion
The SSA was examined for the concentration of heavy metals and phosphorous through digestion.
The results from the ICP were converted from mg/l to mg/kg dry matter by using formula 3.11,
with a dilution factor of 1.
The results from the ICP were all within the standard curve, except for phosphorous, which was
above. Although the result of phosphorous is not completely reliable as it was not within the
standard curve, 114000 mg/kg dry ash, corresponds to a phosphorous content of 11%, which
sounds reasonable, as the content in SSA in general is between 5-10% according to Ottosen et al.
(2013).
When comparing concentrations of heavy metals with the limit values from the two acts, it is
noticed that Cd, Cu, Pb, and Zn, exceeded both acts, while Cr exceeded BEK nr 1001. The results
indicate, that in its raw format, the SSA can be used within category 3 from the residual product
act, BEK nr 1672, but not within agriculture.
4.3
Production and testing of specimens
4.3.1
Pre-tests
4.3.1.1
Production method
The pre-production showed that a compression force of 8 kN resulted in the highest bulk density
and fewest air gaps. The bulk densities were in the range of 1949-1962 kg/m3 for the different test
setups. The height of the specimens was 63mm, when using 350 g of moist soil. This corresponded
to a compaction ratio of 2, and the height was within the recommendation for static compression
to limit the variability of the density throughout the specimen (Fabbri et al., 2022; Houben &
62
==PAGE:70==
Guillaud, 1994).
Figure 4.8 shows test specimens compacted with 4 kN, 6 kN, and 8 kN at a rate of 50mm/min.
The figure show that by increasing the compaction force, the specimen became more uniform.
Figure 4.8: From left: RS-RAW compressed with 4kN, 6kN, and 8kN.
The pre-production of test specimens furthermore showed that the natural water content was too
high. The high water content resulted in uneven surfaces, shrinkage, and large air gaps. Figure 4.9
shows a comparison of the natural red clay (RS-RAW) and the modified version with additional
sand (RS-REF).
Figure 4.9: RS-RAW vs. RS REF with natural water content (top). RS-RAW (wc 14%wt) vs. RS-REF
(wc 12%wt) (bottom)
63
==PAGE:71==
The top figure shows the specimens at their natural water content, while the bottom shows the
specimens at their optimal moisture content. Both sets are compacted with 8 kN.
A rate of 50mm/min was found to be very slow, thus it was increased to 100mm/min for the final
specimens. The compaction was not increased further, as the maximum force of the machine was
10 kN. When having too moist soil, and compressing with 9.5 kN, the machine would exceed its
max, and close down.
The final specimens were compacted at their optimal moisture content with 8 kN at a rate of
100mm/min.
4.3.1.2
Optimal moisture content
The specimens from the OMC can be seen in figure 4.10, while figure 4.11 presents the water
content as a function of the density for the different soil mixes. Both a theoretical density and the
true density are plotted in figure 4.11. The theoretical density relates to the water content added
to the soil, and the true density relates to the actual measured water content in each mix. The
corresponding regression lines for the graphs are listed in appendix C.
64

Figure 4.10: Specimens from the optimal moisture content. The percentage shows the amount of water
added.

Figure 4.11: Optimal moisture content of the different mixes

Compared to the pre-tests with the raw soil (RS-RAW) at its natural moisture content, the test

condition. This indicated that the specimens were better compacted.

From table 4.9 it can be seen that the mix with no binders (RS-REF) resulted in the greatest
dry density. It is thereby expected to have a good compaction and a high compressive strength
compared to the other specimens. The lowest dry density was theoretically achieved by the mix
with quicklime as the only binder (RS-CaO), while the actual (true) lowest density was achieved
by the mix containing both quicklime and SSA as a binder (RS-CaO-SSA). It is noticeable that
the dry density of the specimen with SSA as the only binder (RS-SSA) also resulted in a greater
dry density compared to the once mixed with quicklime. But the dry density were still lower than
the reference mix with no binder (RS-REF).
From figure 4.11 it is noticed that the soil mix containing quicklime in general decreased the true
water content. When comparing RS-CaO-Qz and RS-CaO-SSA, it was not as prominent for the
one with SSA. For the mixes without quicklime as a binder, there was almost no difference in
the theoretical and true water content. This indicated that a reaction had occurred between the
quicklime and the water during the production time, as well as the SSA had not.

==PAGE:75==
The OMC, which gave the theoretical highest dry density were chosen as the water content for
the production of the test specimens, except for RS-REF which were produced at a higher water
content due to a calculation mistake.
4.3.1.3
Linear shrinkage of specimens
To evaluate whether it should be the raw soil (RS-RAW) or the modified soil with extra sand
(RS-REF) that should be mixed with binder and used for further testing, the linear shrinkage was
examined from the two soil mixes at the different water contents from the determination of OMC.
The shrinkage was measured after 11 days of drying in a climate chamber at 23°C and 65%RH.
The shrinkage is compared in figure 4.12.
Figure 4.12: Linear shrinkage at different water contents after 11 days of drying at 23°C and 65%RH
All measurements can be found in appendix C.
From figure 4.12 it is clear that the shrinkage increased with the moisture content. It is also clear
that the raw soil with no extra sand shrunk more than the modified soil, which had 15% extra
0-4mm sand added. These results comply with what was expected, but they differ from the linear
shrinkage based on the British Standard which showed that the two soils shrunk equally.
RS-REF was found better for the production of CEB when taking shrinkage into account.
68
==PAGE:76==
4.3.2
Compression tests
All specimens had the same type of fracture. The fractures either developed vertically from the
top or the bottom of the specimens, as seen in figure 4.13. Whether fractures developed from the
bottom or the top may depend on which end of the specimen was compacted by the moving piston
(Fabbri et al., 2022).
Figure 4.13: Compression test of RS-SSA, cured at 23°C
The fractures furthermore developed around the specimen, making an outer layer slip, as can be
seen in figure 4.14. When lightly hitting the specimen, the soil fell off, leaving the specimen in
a shape of an hourglass. The hourglass shape is similar to concrete cylinder specimens under
uniaxial compression when the height = the diameter (Kim & Yi, 2002).
Figure 4.14: RS-REF, cured at 23°C (left). RS-SSA, cured at 23°C (right).
69
==PAGE:77==
The results from the compression test can be expressed as stress-strain curves. The stress-strain
curves for specimens cured at 23°C and 40°C are illustrated in figure 4.15 and 4.16, respectively.
Average values were used. The stress-displacement curves from the compression tests can be seen
in appendix C, and the compressive strength for each specimen can be seen in table C.3 in the
appendix.
Figure 4.15: Stress-strain curve for specimens cured at 23°C

4.3.2.1
Compressive strength
The stress-strain curves in figure 4.15 show that the specimens without quicklime reached the
highest compressive strength and that there was a clear difference between the specimens, whether
they contained quicklime or not. The reference soil (RS-REF) and the soil with SSA (RS-SSA)
reached the same compressive strength.
When looking at 4.16 there was a less distinct difference between specimens with and without
quicklime. RS-REF and RS-SSA still reached the highest compressive strength. Of the specimens
containing quicklime, the one with quartz reached the highest compressive strength. There is a
clear difference between the compressive strength when looking at the curing temperature and
the specimens with quicklime. The specimens cured at 40°C got a higher compressive strength
than the ones cured at 23°C. There was no clear difference between RS-REF and RS-SSA when
looking at the different curing temperatures.
It was expected that the reference soil (RS-REF) should perform worse than the other mixes, as
the silica and alumina in the clay should react with the binders, creating C-S-H and C-A-H, which
would increase the density of the soil and thereby the strength. The hypothesis was additionally,
that the combination of CaO and SSA, would increase the reactivity of the SSA, increasing the
strength of RS-CaO-SSA compared to RS-SSA. Furthermore, to check if the SSA had reacted with
the CaO, RS-CaO-Qz was included in the test, as the quartz is inert, and should not react with CaO
or the clay minerals, expecting it to have a lower compressive strength than RS-CaO-SSA.
71
==PAGE:79==
It can be concluded that when quicklime was used, curing temperature had a significant influence
on the compressive strength, which may also enhance the durability of the specimens (Minke,
2012), although this was not examined.
Figure 4.17 shows the relationship between the compressive strength and the measured (true) dry
density of all specimens tested.
Figure 4.17: Relationship between the compressive strength, 𝜎and the measured dry density.
The graph shows that the specimens without quicklime both reached a high dry density and a
high compressive strength, compared to the specimens with quicklime. The specimens containing
quicklime all ended with a lower dry density. Among the specimens with quicklime, it is clear that
the curing condition of 40°C increased the strength, while the dry density stayed the same. Among
the specimens without quicklime, the dry density and compressive strength were the same for the
two curing conditions. The specimens RS-SSA reached a higher dry density than the reference
specimens (RS-REF). RS-CaO-SSA got the lowest dry density among the different soil mixes.
72
==PAGE:80==
When comparing the specimens without quicklime, the specimens with SSA showed a tendency of
better compaction, as a higher dry density, in general, was achieved compared to the reference soil,
but the water content for RS-REF and RS-SSA were 14% and 12%, respectively, thus the RS-SSA
contained less water from the beginning. This could be an explanation for the dry density being
slightly different between RS-REF and RS-SSA. If the water content had been the same in the two
mixes, the dry density would maybe be the same, thus it is difficult to state whether it is due to
the SSA or the lower initial water content, that made the soil compact better. The specimens with
quicklime and SSA did not indicate that the SSA increased the density since this soil mix achieved
the lowest dry density.
4.3.2.2
Young’s modulus of elasticity
The stiffness of the specimens was calculated based on Young’s modulus of elasticity using the
secant of the stress-strain curves in figure 4.15 and 4.16.
From the calculation of Young’s modulus of elasticity, it can be seen that the reference soil (RS-
REF) and the soil with SSA (RS-SSA) had the same stiffness despite the different curing condi-
tions. For the specimens with quicklime, Young’s modulus of elasticity was lower for the speci-
mens cured at 23°C than the ones cured at 40°C. This is also visible in figure 4.15 and 4.16 when
looking at the slope of the curves. The steeper the curve is, the stiffer the soil mix. Compared to
conventional construction materials such as concrete, steel, and fired bricks, which have a mod-
ulus of elasticity of 30 GPa, 210 GPa, and 1-30 GPa, respectively, it must be concluded that the
soil mixes are very elastic compared to conventional building materials (Gottfredsen & Nielsen,
2015).

content at the compression test, are listed in table 4.11.

The theoretical water content determines the water content added to the soil mixes during mixing,
while the true water content is the measured water content after the production of cylinders, which
is the water content expected to be in the cylinders when the curing begins.
In the reference soil (RS-REF) and the soil containing SSA (RS-SSA), the true water content was
similar to the theoretical water content. For the specimens with quicklime (RS-CaO, RS-CaO-
SSA, RS-CaO-Qz) all of the measured water contents were lower than the initial water content,
which was added during mixing. This indicates that a reaction between the CaO and the water
had occurred during production time, which reduced the water content in the specimens. This was
also noticeable when looking at the initial density of the specimens produced for curing at 40°and
23°C. As the specimens for 40°C were produced first, these specimens reached a higher initial
density than the specimens produced for 23°C, as the production time of the 40°C specimens took
more than one hour, thus the CaO in the specimens for 23°C had already reacted with the water
before the production of 23°C specimens. The calculation of all initial densities can be seen in
appendix C.
The table also shows the water content in the specimens when they were tested. The water content
was measured to be between 3% and 4% and was based on one specimen from each curing condi-
tion. Even though the specimens began with different water contents, the water content was found
to be approximately the same after drying.
4.3.2.4
Linear shrinkage and change in density
The height and mass of the specimens were measured after 14 days of curing at their respective
temperatures, and after 21 days when the specimens had dried in the climate chamber for 7 days.
Figure 4.18 shows the average linear shrinkage and the change (increase) of the specimens’ dry
74
==PAGE:82==
density in percentage when cured at 23°C and 40°C.
Figure 4.18: Linear shrinkage and change in density of specimens cured at 23°C and 40°C
From the graphs above it is clear that the reference soil (RS-REF) had the greatest shrinkage in
percentage, independently of the curing temperature. In comparison, the specimens with SSA
had the smallest shrinkage in percentage. This may be related to that the SSA can work as a filler,
similar to sand, which can reduce shrinkage (Payá et al., 2019; Minke, 2012). It is interesting to see
that the specimens containing quicklime, which were cured at 40°C, experienced a slight increase
in height during the time of curing, and then began to shrink when they were set to dry. The
same happened for RS-CaO-SSA at 23°C but was less prominent. The expansion can be caused
by hydration products or the formation of gypsum, which could be analyzed by XRD or SEM.
The expansion may be so little, that an XRD and SEM cannot confirm the reaction products. The
linear shrinkage of the specimens was found to be less than 2% for the reference soil (RS-REF),
and less than 1% for the other mixes.
The large shrinkage of the reference (RS-REF), cannot be explained by the water content, as
75
==PAGE:83==
the water content when testing was about the same, and the water content during mixing and
production was lower for the reference than the other soil mixes. One explanation can be, that the
clay content is higher in the reference, as the binder reduces the relative clay content by reducing
the amount of raw soil present in the specimen (Minke, 2012).
The change in dry density was found to be between 1% and 2% for both curing conditions. The
dry density remained the same during the first 14 days, with a change of up to 0.5%, while curing,
as the specimens were sealed in plastic films and plastic bags, which prevented the specimens
from drying while curing. After curing the specimens were set to dry, which increased the dry
density. At the curing condition of 23°C, the RS-REF, RS-CaO, and RS-CaO-SSA had the greatest
increase of dry density, while RS-REF, RS-CaO-Qz, and RS-CaO had the greatest increase of dry
density when cured at 40°C. For both curing conditions, the dry density of RS-SSA changed the
least. When compared with the linear shrinkage, it is also the RS-SSA that had the lowest percent
shrinkage, thus the soil mix seemed to be relatively stable from production.
4.3.3
pH of test specimens
Mold was noticed on the specimens without quicklime (RS-REF and RS-SSA) after curing. It was
therefore interesting to check the pH of the test specimens, to see if that could explain why the
mold only was on those two soil mixes. The pH was measured on specimens cured at 23°C.
Table 4.12: The pH value in the test specimens

The measured pH from the test specimens showed a difference in pH of the specimens which had
mold growth and the ones that did not. The specimens with mold had a neutral pH, while the
specimens without mold had a very alkaline pH. The alkalinity came from the quicklime.
A study by Nascimento et al. (2020) found that the activity of a fungi from the Caatinga soil in
Brazil, was highest at a pH of 9 and a temperature of 40°C. This may explain why mold growth
only were detected on the specimens with a pH close to neutral.
76
==PAGE:84==

The pH of RS-SSA was about neutral with a pH of 8, while the RS-CaO-SSA was alkaline with

increase the amount of soluble salts (Fabbri et al., 2022).

did though indicate that leaching of Cd, Cr, Cu, Pb, Zn, chloride, and nitrate from RS-SSA was

product act, BEK nr 1672. From the RS-CaO-SSA, only Cu was detected by the ICP, as the
results for the other heavy metals and anions were below standard curve values. The RS-CaO-
SSA exceeded the limit value for the leaching of Cu. Since one of the heavy metals exceeded the
limit values, the soil can be used for purposes within Category 3 according to the residual product
act, BEK nr 1672.
4.3.5
Sorption isotherms
The samples were measured at three different relative humidities, 33%, 75%, and 94% during
adsorption and desorption. It took 28 days to execute the test. The samples were fast at stabilizing,
which may be because the samples were crushed instead of solid, which increased the surface area
of the samples.
The RH and temperature were monitored by using an EasyLog datalogger, but due to technical
issues, the data could not be extracted. Figure 4.19 is a graph of the readings from the datalogger,
which were placed within the climate chamber. The reading of the RH was performed once a day
when the mass of the samples was measured.
Figure 4.19: Monitoring of the relative humidity in the climate chamber
The RH seemed to be relatively stable, except on day 24, where the climate chamber suddenly
increased from 79%RH to 88%RH. The samples had stabilized except for one, thus it was decided
to change the relative humidity to 33%, in case the climate chamber would continue increasing
78
==PAGE:86==
when it was set to 75%. The relative humidities measured by the datalogger, had in general
been approximately 2% RH higher than the one the climate chamber was set to, thus a relative
humidity set to 33%RH was measured by the datalogger to be closer to 35%RH, 95%RH were
closer to 97%RH, and the 75%RH was approximately 75%RH during adsorption, but 78%RH
during desorption.
The temperature was not monitored, as it was intended that the datalogger would provide the data,
and monitoring the temperature at each weighing would have required that the climate chamber
were opened more than desired.
Figure 4.20 reports the sorption isotherms for the different soil mixes.
Figure 4.20: Sorption isotherms of specimens
From the sorption isotherms, it can be seen that the hysteresis phenomenon occurred. The phe-
nomenon was greater for the soil mixes with quicklime. The greatest difference between RS-CaO
at 33%RH was 1% moisture content, on the contrary, was the greatest difference between the
moisture content of RS-REF at 75% 0.4%. The hysteresis phenomenon is associated with the ink-
bottle mechanism, which indicates that it was easier for water vapor to enter the porous network
than leaving it, and this was more prominent for the samples with quicklime. If the soil has a
79
==PAGE:87==
compact structure it may be more difficult for the water vapor to leave (El Fgaier et al., 2016).
The moisture content in the samples reached 3.5-4% at 94%RH. The samples from RS-CaO and
RS-CaO-Qz reached the highest change in moisture content, and their behavior was shown to be
very similar. The RS-REF and RS-SSA had the lowest change in moisture content but were also
behaving in the same way. In between were the samples from RS-CaO-SSA. The results indicated
that the CaO increased the ability to absorb and release moisture, while the SSA reduced this
ability of the loam.
80
==PAGE:88==
5
|
Discussion
5.1
Suitability of the soil and SSA based on the characterization
5.1.1
Particle size distribution and Atterberg limits
The soil characterization was performed to figure out the behavior and suitability of the loams for
the production of CEB. The liquid limit, plasticity index, and particle size distribution indicated
that the red clay loam with sand would be the most suitable, compared to the other loams, as its
particle size distribution was closest to the recommended zone from Houben & Guillaud (1994).
The loam still did not completely comply with the zone. A theoretical modification of the soil,
using 0-4mm sand and 0-2mm sand showed that the red clay loam with sand would fit better within
the recommended zone from Houben & Guillaud (1994).
It can also be discussed whether the SSA itself could have modified the soil. The particle size
distribution of the SSA and Qz were very similar and was within silts and fine sand, and could
therefore also help with modifying the soil. The particle size distribution of the SSA and Qz are
plotted together with the particle size distribution of the different loams in figure 5.1.
81
==PAGE:89==
Figure 5.1: Particle size distribution for soils, sand, SSA, and Qz with recommendation for CEB
The option of using the SSA to modify the particle size distribution of the red clay loam is sup-
ported by Lynn et al. (2018) who found that SSA predominantly consists of silt and fine sand
particles, which results in a particle density curve similar to filler or fine aggregate, which also is
the case for the SSA examined in this thesis.
The two soils, the natural red clay loam and the modified red clay loam are compared with soils
used in research, to see if the soil properties are similar, to evaluate whether the soil is suitable
for producing CEB, although it has been difficult to find research combining SSA and CEB. Lin
et al. (2007) and Kadhim et al. (2022) both examine SSA in relation to soil improvement of soft
cohesive subgrade soil used for geotechnical applications. Lin et al. (2007) combine the SSA
with lime. Boussaa et al. (2023) have examined the use of a wood biomass ash as a stabilizer in
compressed earth blocks, and Malkanthi et al. (2020) have stabilized compressed earth blocks by
using lime and cement. The comparison can be seen in figure 5.2 and 5.3.
82
==PAGE:90==
Figure 5.2: Comparison of clay, silt and sand fractions.
Figure 5.2 lists the fraction of clay, silt and sand used in the four different researches. The clay
content in the soil is important as it is the binder of the soil. When modifying the red clay loam,
the clay content reduced to about 15%. This is in between the clay content of Malkanthi et al.
(2020) and Boussaa et al. (2023) who both produced CEB in relation with their research. This
could indicate that the clay content in the modified red clay loam is sufficient for production of
CEB. The clay content in Kadhim et al. (2022) and Lin et al. (2007) are higher, which is expected,
as they work with soft cohesive subgrade soil. In addition, the modified red clay loam can be
characterized as a sandy loam, where the loam in Boussaa et al. (2023) is silty. A silty loam can
reach higher compressive strengths than a sandy loam (Minke, 2012).
When comparing the distribution of clay, silt, and sand fraction, and the liquid limit, and plasticity
index of the natural red clay loam and the modified red clay loam with table 2.1 from section 2.1.2,
it is only the plasticity index that fall outside of the recommendation of the properties of stabilized
CEB in standards and codes.
83
==PAGE:91==
Figure 5.3: Comparison of PL, LL, and PI.
When looking further into the Atterberg limits, the liquid limit of the red clay loams are similar
to the liquid limit of Malkanthi et al. (2020) and Bousaa et al. (2022), and the plasticity index
is higher than the plasticity index of Malkanthi et al. (2020) and Bousaa et al. (2023). A low
PI should increase workability, which both Malkanthi et al. (2020) and Bousaa et al. (2023)
have. The red clay loams could therefore potentially benefit from a lower plasticity index. This
could be achieved by reducing the difference between the PL and the LL, either by increasing
the clay content, or reducing the content of sand, by maybe increasing the content of silt. The
comparison of the soil properties is done in relation to soils that are intended to be stabilized.
Thus the comparison of Atterberg limits is rather to see what soil properties the red clay loam
should have to produce stabilized CEB, rather than unstabilized CEB, as the objective of the thesis
is to examine the effect of the SSA when used as a stabilizer.
5.1.2
Linear shrinkage
The linear shrinkage of the red clay loam and the modified version was found to be 11%. Fur-
thermore, the results of the clay mineralogy by XRD, showed that the red clay loam contains
smectites, which could be montmorillonite. Montmorillonite has a tendency to swell, which can
84
==PAGE:92==
increase the risk of cracks when CEB dries. This risk should be reduced in the modified version
since the addition of sand would reduce the clay content, and thereby the shrinkage ratio (Minke,
2012).
The linear shrinkage of the two loams seems high in comparison with figure 5.4 from Minke
(2012). In figure 5.4, the linear shrinkage is 5% for a loam with 50% clay and 50% silt. The
shrinkage reduces when additional sand is added.
Figure 5.4: Linear shrinkage of two soils with
50% clay and 50% silt as a function of increased
sand content (Minke, 2012)
Figure 5.5: Linear shrinkage as a function of the
addition of normal or coarse sand (Minke, 2012)
Figure 5.5 from Minke (2012) indicates that the addition of coarse sand (1-2mm) reduces the
shrinkage more than when adding a normal sand (0-2mm). This indicates, that if wishing to
reduce the shrinkage of the specimen, it is better to modify the soil with the 0-4mm sand, than the
one with 0-2mm sand.
85
==PAGE:93==
Figure 5.6: Comparison of linear shrinkage
In Minke (2012) it is also evident that the type of clay minerals influences the linear shrinkage.
In figure 5.6 is shown the linear shrinkage of two clay minerals from Minke (2012), which are
compared with the linear shrinkage of the red clay loam and the modified version. The bentonite
from Minke (2012) consists of 71% montmorillonite and 16% illite. Both the kaolinite and the
bentonite are thinned with sand with 0-2mm grains. The bentonite clay with the swelling clay
minerals reaches a linear shrinkage of 18%, while the one with kaolinite reaches a shrinkage of
6%. The linear shrinkage of the two red clays, are in between, indicating that there are some
swelling clay minerals in the loam, which was supported by the XRD.
5.1.3
Stabilization
Cement and bitumen are useful stabilizers when having loam with less clay, and lime is preferable
when having clayey loams. But it also depends on the type of clay minerals present in the loam.
Cement is most effective with kaolinite and lime with montmorillonite (Minke, 2012). When look-
ing at the soil properties of the red clay loam and the modified version, the presence of swelling
clay minerals may favor the addition of lime as a stabilizing agent, but the relatively low clay
content may favor cement as a stabilizing agent. When looking at the Atterberg limits, figure 5.7
from Minke (2012), it indicates that lime is useful for stabilizing the red clay loam. The red dot
is the placement of the red clay loam in the chart. Only one dot has been added, as the Atterberg
limits of the natural red clay loam and the modified version are very similar.
86
==PAGE:94==
Figure 5.7: Suitable stabilizer based on Atterberg limits (Minke, 2012)
SSA or other ashes are not included as a stabilizing agent in Minke (2012). But since SSA can
have a pozzolanic reaction similar to lime, the SSA might be suitable to stabilize the CEB made
of the red clay loam.
In figure 5.8, Minke (2012) shows the compressive strength as a function of the percentage of lime
added to different types of loams. When modifying the red clay loam, it becomes a sandy loam.
87
==PAGE:95==
Figure 5.8: Compressive strength as a function of lime content.
The figure shows an optimum of 4% added lime for clayey loam, while the silty and sandy loam
show an optimum at 6%. Houben & Guillaud (1994) suggest a minimum of 5% slaked lime, for
stabilizing a sandy loam. Kadhim et al. (2022) found an optimum of 10% SSA for soft cohesive
subgrade soil, Lin et al. (2007) found that 8% sewage sludge and lime would improve the basic
properties of the soft subgrade soil, and Aparna (2022) suggest an amount of 7-10% SSA after
reviewing different research papers. Malkanthi et al. (2020) found an optimum of 10% lime when
stabilizing CEB. Based on the above literature and research, the addition of 5-10% stabilizers
seems applicable to improve the compressive strength of the tested CEB.
5.2
Performance of test specimens
The objective was to figure out how the SSA would influence the performance of the CEB. The
test specimens with the different addition of stabilizing agents, were tested for OMC, compressive
strength, and sorption capacity.
5.2.1
SSA impact on optimal moisture content
The OMC was noticed to increase when adding quicklime, but not when adding SSA, and the dry
density increased when adding SSA and decreased when adding CaO. Multiple studies have shown
88
==PAGE:96==
that the OMC should increase and the dry density decrease with the increase of SSA (Aparna,
2022). But other studies have also shown the opposite, as is the case in this project. This was a
surprise during mixing, as it was expected that the addition of SSA would require more water than
the reference soil.
5.2.2
SSA impact on compression strength
The tests reached a compressive strength between 2.19 MPa and 3.69 MPa, where the lowest
compressive strength was achieved by RS-CaO when cured at 23°C, and the highest was achieved
by RS-SSA when cured at 40°C, closely followed by RS-REF. This contradict with what was
expected.
The study by Lin et al. (2007), Malkanthi et al. (2020), and Kadhim et al. (2022), indicated that
when using SSA/lime, lime, or SSA for stabilization, the compressive strength would increase.
Based on the literature, the hypothesis was that the reference soil would perform worse, as it was
not stabilized.
The results may have been influenced by the preparation method, the particle density and packing
(Minke, 2012), thus the red clay loam, may have been of a too good quality, where stabiliza-
tion was unnecessary. Similar to Wiehle et al. (2022), who reached relatively high compressive
strengths of 2.38-4.7 MPa without stabilization. The general compressive strength of loam for
buildings is 1.96-4.90 MPa (Minke, 2012), which corresponds to the results from the different soil
mixes, thus the SSA did not increase the strength compared to the general compressive strength
that is possible to achieve.
5.2.2.1
Influence of curing
Although the RS-SSA reached the highest compressive strength, the tests did not show a clear
indication that the mechanical performance was enhanced by the use of SSA as a binder. When
comparing RS-CaO-SSA and RS-CaO-Qz, when cured at 23 °C, the RS-CaO-SSA reached the
highest compressive strength. But when the soils had cured at 40°C, RS-CaO-Qz had reached the
highest compressive strength. From this, it is hard to state whether the SSA influenced the com-
pressive strength, when mixed with quicklime, as contradictory conclusions are made depending
on the curing condition. It may be because of the short curing time of 14 days, as the SSA is
slow at reacting. There might not be sufficient evidence to say that there is a significant difference
89
==PAGE:97==
between the results, as average values have been used based on a relatively small sample of five
test specimens.
The study by Kadhim et al. (2022) did not show a large difference in the compressive strength
between the 14 days of curing and 28 days of curing when using 10% SSA, whereas Lin et al.
(2007) showed an increase in the compressive strength even after the 28 days of curing. The test
with SSA/Lime of 8% increased from 80 kPa after 14 days of curing to 140 kPa after 100 days of
curing.
A longer curing time would maybe indicate differences between the specimen’s compressive
strength, making it easier to see if the SSA had reacted and whether it can improve the mechanical
performance of CEB.
5.2.2.2
Reactivity of the SSA
The hypothesis was that the SSA would create C-S-H and C-A-H, which could reduce the porosity
and increase the density and thus the strength of the specimens (Aparna, 2022). But as the SSA
itself contains both calcium oxide (C), silicate (S), and aluminum oxide (A) (Dhir et al., 2017a),
it can be hard to tell whether the SSA reacts with itself or the soil minerals, and which of the
constituents are more reactive. When stabilizing with lime, the alkaline condition favour the silica
and alumina in the soil to react with the Ca+ ions from the lime, to form calcium silicate hydrates
and aluminum silicate hydrates (Dhir et al., 2017a). The pH of the soil is about neutral, while the
SSA increases the pH slightly, making the soil more alkaline. This may increase the reactivity of
the soil and the SSA.
It requires further testing to know the mineralogy after curing, and the content of hydrates and
reactants that have occurred to exactly know whether the SSA has reacted with the soil, and if the
CaO has increased or helped the reactivity of the SSA. The compression test did not indicate a
reaction between the SSA and the soil, or an increased reactivity when mixed with quicklime, thus
it is difficult to state whether the SSA can be used for stabilization and enhancing the mechanical
and physical properties of the soil. On the other hand it can be stated that the SSA did not impair
the material properties, as the compressive strength remained the same as the reference, and it
reduced the shrinkage of the specimens, thus the SSA can maybe be considered to be used as
a substitution for sand, which is also considered as a scarce resource and a way to reduce the
depletion of natural resources (UNEP, 2014).
90
==PAGE:98==
5.2.3
SSA impact on the hygrothermal performance
The sorption capacity of earth building materials should be high compared to other construction
materials. A high sorption capacity enables the material to balance the indoor climate by absorbing
and releasing moisture when the relative humidity changes (El Fgaier et al., 2016).
The results of the hygrothermal performance of the samples of the different specimens, showed
that the specimens with quicklime reached a higher moisture content at 94% RH, than the ref-
erence soil (RS-REF) and the soil with SSA alone (RS-SSA). It also showed that the difference
between adsorption and desorption were greater in the specimens with quicklime, than the once
without. The samples with quicklime might therefore be better at regulating the indoor climate.
It furthermore indicated that the SSA could have a negative influence on the hygrothermal perfor-
mance, as the moisture content of RS-CaO-SSA was lower than the moisture content of RS-CaO
and RS-CaO-Qz.
For the sake of clarity only the samples containing SSA are presented in the following graphs,
when comparing the results with sorption isotherms from litterature and research.
In figure 5.9 the two samples containing SSA are compared with different loams from Minke
(2012).
91
==PAGE:99==
Figure 5.9: Comparison of sorption isotherms of different loams with the sorption isotherms of the samples
containing SSA.
The figure shows that the samples containing SSA fall between the equilibrium moisture contents
of a silty loam and a sandy loam, which sounds reasonable as the soil was thinned with extra sand.
However, the samples do not reach a moisture content as high as the loam brick. The sorption
isotherms can depend on the clay minerals, where a higher moisture content is reached when the
loam contains montmorillionite (Minke, 2012). This is illustrated both by El Fgaier et al. (2016) in
figure 2.3, where brick B contained montmorillonite and by Minke (2012) that illustrates in figure
2.4 that the bentonite clay containing 70% montmorillonite reached a higher moisture content than
the non-swelling clay kaolinite. Minke (2012) does not mention what type of clay minerals the
loam brick contains, thus it is difficult to know why this brick reaches a higher moisture content
than both the bricks from El Fgaier et al. (2016) with a maximum moisture content of 3.5%, and
the samples of RS-SSA and RS-CaO-SSA.
92
==PAGE:100==
In figure 5.10 the two samples with SSA are compared with conventional materials such as con-
crete and fired bricks, and an unfired clay brick from the study by El Fgaier et al. (2016). The
sorption isotherms of the three materials are also presented in figure 2.3, 2.5, and 2.6 in section
2.1.3.
Figure 5.10: Comparison of sorption isotherms of different building materials with the sorption isotherms
of the samples containing SSA.
From the figure it is noticed that the moisture content of fired bricks is very low compared to
the CEB samples. The hysteresis phenomenon for the fired brick is greatest at the high relative
humidities, but the difference is not greater than 0.2%. The sorption isotherm of the concrete
is more similar to the CEB samples, but it reaches a higher moisture content at 95%RH. The
hysteresis phenomenon is also greater for the concrete example than the CEB samples. When
comparing the CEB samples with the unstabilized unfired clay brick by El Fgaier et al. (2016), it
is noticed that they reaches the same moisture content of 3.5%. The CEB samples differ from the
unstabilized unfired clay brick by El Fgaier et al. (2016), by having a higher moisture content at
93
==PAGE:101==
the relative humidities lower than 75% RH.
The sorption isotherms conducted in this thesis showed an increase of moisture content for stabi-
lized specimens, which contradict with results obtained by McGregor et al. (2014), where sorption
isotherms for CEB specimens stabilized with 4% or 8% cement or lime, all showed a lower mois-
ture content at all relative humidities compared to unstabilized CEB. The CEB in McGregor et al.
(2014) consisted of 50% brick clay and 50% fine sand.
It must be taken into account, that the sorption isotherms may have been executed on different
types of samples. The samples used in this thesis were crushed to a size smaller than 4mm, while
the samples from El Fgaier et al. (2016) were done on solid bricks. The type of samples used in
Gottfredsen & Nielsen (2015) is unknown. It might not have an influence since the results used
for the sorption isotherms are obtained when the samples have reached equilibrium at the different
relative humidities. Only the time to obtain equilibrium may be affected by the type of sample, as
it may increase the time for samples of larger bricks.
The samples used in this project were fast at reaching equilibrium, which may be attributed to the
fact that the specimens were crushed, which increased the surface area of the sample.
From the comparison it can thus be concluded that the equilibrium moisture content of the soil
mixes tested corresponds to sorption isotherms of similar earth materials. Both from El Fgaier
et al. (2016) and Minke (2012). Compared to other conventional building materials, the samples
from the CEB performed better than fired bricks, but performed similar to a concrete with a w/c
ratio of 0.66, and degree of hydration of 96%. Between the results obtained in this thesis, it seems
as the SSA reduces the equilibrium moisture content when mixed with CaO, while it did not
change the hygrothermal performance, when compared to the reference specimen, as the sorption
isotherms of the RS-SSA and RS-REF were similar.
5.3
Environmental aspects of using SSA in CEB
When mixing or creating a new building material, it is important to consider the end-of-life sce-
nario. It is therefore important to examine if mixing clean earth material with SSA, will cause any
harm to the environment. In this context, pH, conductivity, and leaching were examined to evalu-
ate the risk at the end-of-life of the CEB when it is mixed with SSA, and the total concentration
of heavy metals in dry matter of the raw SSA was examined through digestion.
94
==PAGE:102==
Clayey soil can be considered as a pure material, which can go back to its natural state when water
is added. Since SSA contains pathogens, bacteria and heavy metals, it must be examined how
this will effect the product. The digestion of the raw SSA showed that all limit values from the
act regarding the use of waste for agricultural purposes were exceeded. The residual product act
allows the raw SSA to be used for category 3 purposes. On the contrary, the leaching of heavy
metals and anions from the raw SSA and the test specimens containing SSA was found to be
limited. Only the CEB test specimen containing quicklime and SSA exceeded the limit values
from the residual product act, BEK nr 1672, when looking at Cu, while the raw SSA most likely
exceed the limit value for sulfate. The concentrations of the other heavy metals and anions were
not detected by the IC and ICP.
When the limit values for heavy metals and anions are exceeded for Category 1 and 2, the residual
product act states that the soil and SSA can be used for Category 3 purposes at the end-of-life.
Since heavy metals are not very volatile, the risk of using SSA in construction materials such as
CEB would most likely be limited, but since the limit values from BEK nr 1672 are exceeded, the
CEB should not be used for agricultural purposes at the end-of-life.

When looking at the conductivity and pH of the SSA and test specimens containing SSA, which
are summarized in table 5.1, it can be seen that the soil neutralizes the SSA slightly, as it has a
95
==PAGE:103==
lower pH, closer to neutral, than the raw SSA, while when mixing with quicklime, the quicklime
increases the pH, making the soil very alkaline. When looking at the conductivity, it is lowest
for the RS-SSA, and highest for the soil with quicklime. When looking at both the pH and the
conductivity it is highest for the RS-CaO-SSA, thus when mixing with quicklime, the risk of
leaching will increase. RS-CaO-SSA is the only one of the three materials, where leaching of Cu
exceeds the limit value. The raw SSA was the only one exceeding the limit value for sulfate.
When comparing with a similar SSA from Avedøre in Ottosen et al. (2015), the conductivity
was 1.69 mS/cm and the pH was 10.3. Ottosen et al. (2015) also examined another SSA from
Lundtofte which had a pH of 10.0 and a conductivity of 32.4 mS/cm. The SSA were both alkaline,
but otherwise had different characteristics, thus not all SSA are behaving in the same way. The
content of phosphorous was approximately the same for the two ashes in the study by Ottosen et
al. (2015), with a content of 7-10%. The conductivity of the SSA used for this project is similar
to the Avedøre sewage sludge ash in Ottosen et al. (2015), while the conductivity of RS-SSA is
much lower than the raw ashes, and even the RS-CaO-SSA, which showed the largest conductivity
of what was examined in this project, is much lower than the conductivity of the Lundtofte sewage
sludge ash in Ottosen et al. (2015).
It is interesting to compare the concentration of sulfate in RS-SSA and RS-CaO-SSA. There is a
reduction of the concentration in RS-CaO-SSA compared to the concentration in RS-SSA. This
could indicate that a reaction between the quicklime and the sulfate from the SSA had occurred.
When quicklime reacts with SSA, gypsum can be formed. This may cause an expansion of the
product, which the change in density in section 4.3.2.4 might indicate, but since the concentrations
are limited, this is maybe not noticeable neither visually nor by the use of XRD.
In table 5.3 the concentration of dry matter related heavy metals are compared with concentrations
from sewage sludge ash used in Ottosen et al. (2015) and with the average concentrations of heavy
metals in sewage sludge ash across research represented by Lynn et al. (2018).
96
==PAGE:104==

Both ashes from Ottosen et al. (2015) are untreated, and the concentration of heavy metals were
considered very low for both ashes compared to sewage sludge ashes from other countries (Ottosen
et al., 2015). The concentration of heavy metals in the SSA used for this project were higher than
the concentrations in the sewage sludge ashes examined in Ottosen et al. (2015). The sewage
sludge ashes in Ottosen et al. only exceeds the limit values of Cu in the residual product act, BEK
nr 1672. Lynn et al. (2018) have made an analysis of the concentration of heavy metals in SSA
across litterature. The average values are all greater than the SSA used for this project, except for
Zn. The concentrations from Lynn et al. (2018) are also greater than the concentrations in the
sewage sludge ashes used in Ottosen et al. (2015).
Lynn et al. (2018) concludes that SSA in Europe generally can be classified as non-hazardous, and
that it can be used within e.g. geotechnical applications such as landfill liner, as long as the seepage
rate is reduced. SSA is also suggested to be used as a filler in Controlled Low-Strength Materials.
The leaching from the test specimens examined in this project were also limited, and indicates that
SSA can be used within earth construction materials. The combination of quicklime and SSA may
even reduce the leaching (Taki et al., 2020). Additionally, if pre-tretreating the raw SSA when
removing the phosphorous, Ottosen et al. (2015) presented that electrodialytic sepearation also
can reduce the concentrations of heavy metals. Some concentrations were increased during the
process, but these were assumed to be quite immobile (Ottosen et al., 2015). The electrodialytic
separation could be a way to have a cleaner SSA, with a reduced risk of leaching heavy metals.
97
==PAGE:105==
6
|
Conclusion
The current project seeks to evaluate the possibility of utilizing SSA in compressed earth blocks,
by looking into the SSA influence on the mechanical and physical performance of the compressed
earth blocks. The SSA was evaluated in comparison with specimens stabilized by quicklime alone,
and specimens with quicklime in combination with SSA or quartz. Based on the results from the
conducted experiments the following conclusions can be drawn:
The SSA did not impair the mechanical and physical performance of the CEB. The unstabilized
specimen, and the specimen with 5% SSA reached the highest compressive strength at both 23°C
curing and 40°C curing. The curing temperature showed no influence on the compressive strengths
on these two types, but curing at 40°C increased the strength of specimens stabilized with quick-
lime. Compressive strengths of 3.6-3.7 MPa was reached for the non-stabilized specimen and the
specimen with 5% SSA. The limited difference between the compressive strength of the reference
soil (unstabilized CEB) and the specimen with 5% SSA can maybe be attributed to the slow reac-
tivity of SSA, as a curing time of 14 days is relatively short. Another explaination can be that the
soil could compact sufficiently without the need for stabilization, since the specimens with quick-
lime neither resulted in higher compressive strengths. Further tests must be conducted to confirm
potential reaction products between the SSA and clay minerals.
Based on the compressive strength, the SSA did not show clear evidence on working as a stabilizer,
but results indicated that SSA can reduce shrinkage of clayey soil. With a particle size distribution
similar to fine sand and the ability to reduce shrinkage, the SSA shows a possibility of being
utilized as a substitution for sand by working as a filler or light aggregate in CEB.
When looking at the hygrothermal performance of the different soil mixes, the SSA seemed to
reduce the hygrothermal performance, as the samples with 5% quicklime and 5% SSA reached a
lower moisture content compared to the specimen with 5% quicklime, and the specimen with 5%
quicklime and 5% quartz.
With respect to environmental concerns, the results from leaching showed limited concentrations
of heavy metals and salts, although the specimen with 5% SSA exceeded the limit value of sulfate
from the residual product act BEK 1672, stating that specimens with SSA can be used for purposes
98
==PAGE:106==
within Category 3 at end-of-life.
Overall does the results from the thesis and research indicate that using SSA in earth construction
can help resolving three different problems:
1. The energy and carbon emissions related to construction of residential buildings can be
reduced when building with earth.
2. The depletion of non-renewable ressources such as sand, can be reduced if SSA is used as a
filler in construction materials.
3. Waste generation can be reduced, when utilizing SSA. In addition, when valuable nutrients
can be extracted commercially from the SSA, leaving a residue similar to silica sand, the
full potential of the SSA can be utilized.
99
==PAGE:107==
7
|
Perspectives
The results did not give a clear indication on whether the SSA had reacted with the clay and
lime or not. It would therefore be an idea to perform an XRD, SEM and TGA to be able to
evaluate reaction products, if they have taken place. SSA is very slow at reacting, thus it would
further be an idea to increase the curing time, to see if it would influence the compression strength.
Additionally, since the reference soil performed almost as good as the soil with SSA, and better
than the specimens stabilized with quicklime, it could be an idea to make the same tests on a
different soil, to see if the influence from the SSA is more clear.
In relation with the production method, research write that binders and soil should be mixed when
dry and then wetted when mixing in a kneader, which is the opposite of what was done in this
project. The method could increase the strength according to Minke (2012), but when soaking
the water over night, letting it redistribute in the dry soil, the soil was easier to work with. There
could be done more examinations on how to prepare the soil, as it should have a large influence
in the final product. Thus maybe examine and test the optimum amount of binder, the optimum
holding-time after adding the binder, and maybe also test if increasing the moisture content by
10% of the OMC, would increase the strength, as Minke (2012) states.
Earth-based materials can be used for load-bearing walls in single-storey residential houses, but
due to its low strength and durability issues, it may be more relevant to use for non load-bearing
walls. In this relation it would be interesting to further examine the materials quality in relation
with indoor climate. E.g. by performing a Moisture Buffering Value test, or testing its thermal
conductivity and heat capacity.
Research argue that building with earth and utilizing the SSA, would result in a sustainable use
of materials. In this relation it could be good to perform an LCA, to see if the material is more
environmentally friendly than conventional building materials, as the LCA can include e.g. the
influence from transportation, energy during manufacturing and occupancy, toxicity, and resource
depletion when analysing the material from cradle-to-gate. The analysis could then be compared
with using other binders for stabilization or other conventional building materials.

A.4.2
Sedimentation - Hydrometer method
A.4.2.1
Calibration of hydrometer
The hydrometer is calibrated according to Annex A in DS/CEN ISO/TS 17892-4:2004.
The effective depth, 𝐻𝑟, is calculated by formula A.1
𝐻𝑟= 𝐻+ 0.5(ℎ−𝑉ℎ
900 𝐿)
[mm]
(A.1)
where,
H is the length from neck of bulb to graduation 𝑅ℎ[mm].

𝑅0,𝑐𝑜𝑟takes into account a temperature correction, thus 𝑅0,𝑐𝑜𝑟= 𝑅0 + 𝐶𝑇.
𝑅ℎis the true hydrometer reading, which is calculated by 𝑅ℎ= 𝑅′
ℎ+ 𝐶𝑚, where 𝐶𝑚is a meniscus
correction.
𝐾𝑜𝑙𝑑refers to the fraction smaller than equivalent diameter calculated by using the the old standard
DS 405.8 (1978), which uses a correction factor for particle densities greater than 2.65 𝑀𝑔/𝑚3.
This has been used for joining the sedimentation curve from #0249 with the sieving curve, as the
sedimentation curve for K by using the standard from 2004 or 2016, ended with a fraction greater
than 100%.
𝐾𝑚𝑜𝑑is the modified fraction, which is joined together with the curve from the sieving.

NB: In generel there is a tendency that the theoretical dry density is lower for the 23C specimens than for the 40C specimens. This is most likely due to 
production time. The 40C specimens was produced first, the 23C afterwards. There has most likely been more than one hour between the mixing of the soil 
and binder until production of 23C specimens. The water content have been taken after the production of all 13 specimens, thus it mainly represents the 
water content of the 23C specimens, due to the production time.
